==PAGE:3==
Abstract
The increasing prevalence of self-administered medical devices in home and non-clinical environ-
ments places growing emphasis on ensuring their usability. Devices such as pen injectors must be
designed for intuitive and error-free use to support patient safety, treatment adherence, and regu-
latory compliance. While traditional usability testing methods, such as task performance analysis,
think-aloud protocols, and user interviews, are well established, they often rely on observable be-
haviour and subjective reporting. These approaches may fail to capture more subtle manifestations
of cognitive load, hesitation, or emotional stress that occur during device interaction.
This thesis investigates the potential of Galvanic Skin Response (GSR) as a complementary metric
in formative usability evaluation. GSR provides continuous, real-time physiological data that re-
flects autonomic nervous system activity, offering an objective indication of user arousal. Through
a mixed-methods study conducted in collaboration with Technolution A/S, the research assesses
the usability of pen injectors by triangulating GSR data with video analysis and user-reported
experiences across both standard laboratory and alternative test environments.
The findings demonstrate that GSR can identify moments of heightened arousal that are not always
observable through traditional methods, revealing critical insights into ease of use and usability
in general. By integrating physiological signals into the usability testing framework, this study
contributes to a more comprehensive understanding of human–device interaction. It highlights the
value of multi-modal data in the development of safer and more effective medical devices, aligning
with evolving human factors engineering standards and regulatory expectations.
i
==PAGE:4==
Resume
Brugen af medicinsk udstyr uden for hospitalsmiljøet, særligt peninjektorer, er stigende, hvilket
skærper kravene til, at sådant udstyr er både intuitivt og fejlsikkert. For at sikre patientsikkerhed,
behandlingsloyalitet og opfyldelse af gældende standarder kombinerer dette speciale traditionelle
brugervenlighedstest med målinger af ’Galvanic Skin Response’ (GSR).
GSR leverer kontinuerlige, realtidsdata om det autonome nervesystems aktivitet og giver et ob-
jektivt fingerpeg om brugerens påvirkning (arousal) og kognitive belastning. Gennem et blandet
metode studie udført i samarbejde med Technolution A/S blev peninjektorers brugervenlighed
vurderet både i et traditionelt testmiljø og i et modificeret testmiljø, ved at triangulere GSR-data,
videoanalyse og brugernes egne oplevelser.
Resultaterne viser, at GSR kan afsløre øjeblikke med forhøjet affekt, som ikke altid er synlige gen-
nem traditionel observation eller selvrapporterede data. Ved at inddrage disse fysiologiske signaler
opnår man en mere nuanceret forståelse af menneskers interaktion med peninjektorer, hvilket kan
danne grundlag for målrettede designjusteringer og en generelt sikrere og mere effektiv udvikling af
medicinsk udstyr. Dette studie underbygger vigtigheden af multimodale data i overensstemmelse
med moderne brugervenlighed, standarder og regulatoriske forventninger.

DTU Learning Objectives
• Identify and reflect on technical scientific issues and understand the interaction between the various
components that make up an issue.
• Based on a clear academic profile, apply elements of current research at an international level to
develop ideas and solve problems.
• Master technical scientific methodologies, theories, and tools, and take a holistic view of and
delimit a complex, open issue; place it into a broader academic and societal perspective; and, on
this basis, propose a variety of possible actions while considering sustainability.
• Develop relevant models, systems, technologies, and processes aimed at solving technological prob-
lems.
• Communicate and mediate research-based knowledge both orally and in writing.
• Seek out and engage with leading international research within one’s specialist area.
• Work independently and reflect on one’s own learning, academic development, and specialization.
• Master technical problem-solving at a high level through cross-disciplinary teamwork, and manage
all phases of a project - including preparation of timetables, design, solution, and documentation.
Additional Thesis-Specific Learning Objectives
• Identify the interaction between physiological stress measurement, test environments, and tradi-
tional usability assessment methods to assess the feasibility of GSR integration.
• Apply physiological measurement techniques (using GSR), experimental design principles, and
statistical analysis to evaluate the effectiveness of GSR in detecting stress responses during medical
device interactions.
• Develop methodological improvements for combining GSR with traditional usability evaluation
techniques, considering ethical, regulatory, and practical implementation challenges.
• Present results in a structured, research-based manner while reflecting on the implications of
GSR in usability engineering, its potential impact on MedTech development, and future research
directions.

==PAGE:7==
Definitions
Close call: Case in which a user almost commits a use error while performing a task, but recovers
in time to avoid making the use error [2].
Correct use: Normal use without use error [1].
Ease of use: The degree to which a device’s user interface enables its intended users to safely,
effectively, and efficiently achieve their specific goals with minimal cognitive and physical effort,
while requiring minimal training [1].
Effectiveness: The device allows users to complete critical tasks accurately, reducing the likeli-
hood of use errors that could compromise safety [1].
Efficiency: Tasks can be performed quickly and with minimal unnecessary steps, conserving both
time and cognitive resources [1].
Formative Evaluation: User interface evaluation conducted with the intent to explore user
interface design strengths, weaknesses, and unanticipated use errors. It is generally performed
iteratively [1].
Galvanic: Galvanic refers to electrical currents produced by chemical action [28].
Human Factors: Human factors is the study of how people interact with systems and devices to
improve safety, performance, and user satisfaction [18].
Learnability: New users can rapidly understand and operate the device, reflecting an intuitive
interface that minimises the learning curve [1].
Medical Device: A medical device is any tool (like an instrument or software) created for a
medical purpose (such as diagnosing, preventing, or treating disease) that does not mainly work
through drugs or immune processes [16].
Pen Injector: A medical device that provides a non-electrically-powered, mechanically-operated
method of accurately injecting a dose of medicinal product from a medicinal cartridge, reservoir,
v
==PAGE:8==
or syringe through a manually-inserted single lumen hypodermic needle [21].
Signal Processing: Signal processing involves analysing and manipulating signals (such as elec-
trical or digital signals) to extract useful information or modify the signal [30].
Usability: Characteristic of the user interface that facilitates use and thereby establishes effec-
tiveness, efficiency and user satisfaction in the intended use environment [1].
Usability Testing: Usability testing is the process of evaluating a product or system by having
representative users perform tasks to assess its ease of use and overall user experience [1].
Use Error: User action or lack of user action while using the medical device that leads to a
different result than that intended by the manufacturer or expected by the user [1].
Use Scenario: Specific sequence of tasks performed by a specific user in a specific use environment
and any resulting response of the medical device [1].
User Satisfaction: The overall experience is positive, with a design that meets user expectations
and feels natural, even in high-stress or critical situations [1].

==PAGE:15==
Introduction
1
Introduction
Ensuring the usability of medical devices is a critical concern in healthcare and regulatory con-
texts, where the safety and efficacy of patient-facing technologies directly influence outcomes [1].
As medical devices increasingly transition from professional healthcare settings to home environ-
ments, particularly with self-administered treatments such as those involving pen injectors, the
requirement for intuitive and fail-safe design becomes ever more pronounced. Traditional usability
testing methods, such as task completion analysis, think-aloud protocols, and structured inter-
views, have proven valuable in identifying usability errors and uncovering critical flaws in user
interface (UI) design [36]. However, these methods inherently rely on observable behaviours and
subjective self-reports, which may fail to capture more subtle manifestations of cognitive workload,
emotional strain, or hesitation [45]. In complex or high-stakes usage scenarios, such non-observable
responses may nonetheless carry significant implications for safety, adherence, and device perfor-
mance.
This thesis addresses this gap by exploring the integration of Galvanic Skin Response (GSR) as
a complementary metric in usability evaluation. As an objective indicator of sympathetic ner-
vous system activation, GSR provides real-time measurements of physiological arousal [9]. Conse-
quently, it is increasingly recognised in human factors research as a valuable proxy for assessing
both emotional strain and cognitive workload during user interaction [12, 11]. Unlike traditional
metrics, which are episodic and retrospective, GSR offers continuous data throughout the user
interaction, enabling the detection of stress peaks or sustained arousal that may correlate with
cognitive difficulty, uncertainty, or discomfort, particularly in moments that appear outwardly un-
eventful. By triangulating GSR data with video observations and user-reported experience, this
study seeks to enrich the evaluative framework traditionally applied in medical device usability
studies [5].
The study is situated within a collaborative project between the Technical University of Denmark
and Technolution, focusing on the usability of pen injectors. This medical device exemplifies the
challenges of designing for safe and intuitive use in non-clinical environments. The core objective
is to evaluate whether GSR can provide measurable and actionable insights into user arousal
during device interaction, thereby supporting more comprehensive assessments of ’ease of use’, a
1
==PAGE:16==
Introduction
construct that is crucial in regulatory guidelines yet often ambiguously defined and challenging
to operationalise [1]. Through a series of structured usability tests conducted in both standard
laboratory settings and simulated home environments, this research compares physiological and
observational data to assess the value GSR adds to existing usability testing paradigms.
This thesis contributes to medical device testing methods and broader human factors research by
promoting multi-modal data for deeper usability insights [41]. The findings aim to guide future
design and regulation by showing how physiological signals can highlight key user interactions that
might otherwise be missed, supporting safer, more user-centred medical devices.
1.1
Motivation
In recent years, there has been a growing interest in using physiological signals as complementary
metrics in research. Traditional usability testing methods, such as task performance analysis and
subjective questionnaires, provide valuable information on usability. However, physiological signals
offer an objective and continuous measure of user responses, revealing subconscious reactions that
may not be captured through self-reporting [23].
One particularly promising physiological measure is the Galvanic Skin Response (GSR). As an
indicator of autonomic nervous system activity, GSR reflects changes in skin conductance linked
to emotional and cognitive arousal states [9]. By integrating GSR into usability testing, researchers
can gain deeper insight into user engagement, cognitive load, and emotional responses, providing
a more comprehensive evaluation of usability [11].
In collaboration with Technolution A/S, who conducts usability tests on devices like pen injectors,
this study investigates incorporating GSR as an additional usability metric. As ease of use is vital in
medical devices—especially in healthcare settings—combining traditional testing with GSR offers
a novel way to assess user interaction and reduce stress, supporting patient safety and adherence
[1].
Using both usability tests and physiological data, this study aims to enhance understanding of
usability, contributing to the development of more effective and efficient medical devices. This
collaboration represents a significant opportunity to advance usability research methodologies and
improve the overall quality of usability assessments.
2
==PAGE:17==
Background
2
Background
Usability testing is fundamental for evaluating how users interact with medical devices and iden-
tifying design improvements that enhance safety, efficiency and overall user experience [36]. In
medical device development, products must be intuitive, effective and compliant with regulatory
standards. Recently, integrating physiological metrics such as Galvanic Skin Response (GSR) into
usability evaluations has emerged as a promising approach. GSR provides objective, real-time
measures of user arousal and cognitive load that traditional observational methods may overlook
[9][11]. This section outlines the core principles of usability testing and GSR, reviews available
sensor technologies and their practical applications, and sets the foundation for current research.
2.1
Usability Testing
Usability testing involves a structured evaluation of how effectively users can interact with a prod-
uct or system. Typically conducted in controlled environments, such as usability laboratories, the
process combines quantitative metrics, including task completion times and use errors, with quali-
tative insights from think-aloud protocols and post-task interviews [36]. This method is especially
crucial for medical devices where user errors may have direct safety implications. Regulatory guide-
lines, for example, IEC 62366-1, mandate the integration of human factors testing to reduce the
risk of device misuse [1]. By incorporating physiological measures such as GSR into conventional
usability testing, an approach first articulated by Boucsein (2012) [9] and empirically validated by
Giorgi et al. (2021) [5], researchers can gain a more comprehensive view of usability by capturing
implicit stress responses and cognitive load that might otherwise remain undetected.
2.2
Fundamentals of Galvanic Skin Response
GSR, also known as Electrodermal Activity (EDA), measures changes in the skin’s electrical con-
ductance resulting from sweat gland activity.
The sympathetic nervous system regulates this
process and is closely linked to emotional arousal and cognitive demand [12][68]. When a person
experiences stress or increased cognitive load, activation of the sympathetic nervous system leads
to heightened perspiration and, consequently, elevated skin conductance.
GSR signals are typically decomposed into two components:
3
==PAGE:18==
Background
• Tonic Component (Skin Conductance Level, SCL): This is the baseline level of skin
conductance over extended periods, influenced by factors such as hydration and ambient
temperature.
• Phasic Component (Skin Conductance Response, SCR): These are rapid, transient
changes triggered by specific stimuli, generally occurring within one to five seconds after an
event [68][11].
Key parameters in GSR analysis include response latency (the time delay between stimulus and
peak response), amplitude (the magnitude of the conductance change) and recovery time (the
duration required for the signal to return to baseline) [32]. These metrics provide a detailed, ob-
jective insight into the user’s physiological state, making GSR a valuable tool in both psychological
research and usability testing [31].
2.3
Measurement Technologies and Sensors
Accurate measurement of GSR is essential for effective usability evaluations. GSR sensors vary
in terms of precision, portability, and suitability for different applications. Generally, they can be
grouped into three categories:
• Wearable Sensors: Devices such as the Fitbit Sense and Empatica E4 are designed for con-
tinuous, passive monitoring of stress and emotional states. Although they offer convenience,
their precision is generally lower compared to research-grade instruments [15][17].
• Research-Grade Sensors: Instruments like the Shimmer3 GSR+ and PLUX EDA sen-
sors deliver high-resolution data with high sampling rates, making them ideal for controlled
experimental studies that demand accuracy and reliability [50][60].
• Clinical Biofeedback Devices: These sensors are used in therapeutic settings to monitor
stress and autonomic responses, for example, the Zyto Hand Cradle and TSens GSR [29][64].
Other biosignal devices, such as Electroencephalography (EEG) and Electrocardiography (ECG),
can also assess cognitive and emotional states. However, they generally require more complex
setups and are more intrusive. GSR offers a practical balance between physiological and sensitivity
to emotional and cognitive responses, making it particularly suited to usability testing.
4
==PAGE:19==
Background
Rationale for Selecting the Shimmer3 GSR+:
The Shimmer3 GSR+ was selected for this study due to its high precision and practical usability.
Its ability to provide raw data enables advanced signal processing and real-time analysis, while
Bluetooth wireless connectivity minimises interference with natural user interactions. Although
this study does not use its multi-sensor integration capabilities (with ECG or EMG), the technical
advantages of the Shimmer3 GSR+ render it an excellent choice for assessing physiological arousal
in medical device usability testing [60].
2.4
Applications and Limitations of GSR in Usability Testing
GSR has been applied in diverse fields, including psychology, human-computer interaction, and
medical device evaluation, to assess arousal, anxiety and cognitive load [9][11]. Usability testing
is a complementary metric to traditional measures by providing objective, real-time data that can
reveal high arousal or cognitive overload. Elevated GSR responses correlate with increased task
difficulty or user confusion, flagging potential design flaws.
However, there are several limitations:
• Signal Variability: Individual differences in autonomic activity, hydration, and ambient
conditions can influence conductance readings, making it challenging to establish standard
thresholds for arousal or cognitive load [12].
• External Influences: Movements, electrode displacement, and environmental conditions
may introduce noise into the data. Rigorous experimental control and preprocessing are
required to mitigate these artefacts [11].
• Interpretation Challenges:
While GSR effectively indicates changes in physiological
arousal, it does not inherently distinguish between different emotional states (e.g., stress
versus excitement). Supplementary data, such as heart rate variability or EEG, may be
needed for more nuanced interpretations [32].
• Arousal Valence Ambiguity: GSR cannot differentiate between types of arousal, such as
positive (e.g., excitement) versus negative (e.g. anxiety), since both produce similar increases
in skin conductance. This limits its utility for inferring emotional valence without contextual
or multimodal support [32].
5
==PAGE:20==
Background
• Ethical Considerations: The collection of biometric data raises important privacy and
informed consent issues. It is imperative to ensure participant anonymity and secure storage
of such data [68].
Addressing these challenges is essential to fully leverage the potential of GSR in enhancing usability
evaluations, particularly in the critical context of medical device design.
2.5
Pen Injectors in Usability Evaluation
Pen injectors are medical devices designed for the self-administration of injectable medications and
are widely used by patients with chronic conditions such as diabetes and autoimmune diseases.
These devices are engineered to provide ease of use, precise dosing, and minimise injection-related
anxiety. Features such as ergonomic grips and tactile or audible feedback are incorporated to
support safe and effective medication delivery. Given that pen injectors are often used without
professional supervision, their usability is paramount; even minor errors can have serious reper-
cussions for treatment adherence and patient safety.
This study focuses on pen injectors as a case study in usability evaluation. By integrating tra-
ditional usability testing with GSR measurements, the research aims to obtain a comprehensive
assessment of the device’s design and usability. The combination of observational data and objec-
tive physiological measures enables the detection of subtle variations in arousal and cognitive load
that may indicate usability issues, thereby informing iterative design improvements.
This background section has established the foundational concepts and technologies relevant to this
study. It has outlined the core principles of usability testing, introduced Galvanic Skin Response
(GSR) as a physiological measure of arousal, and explored the opportunities and limitations of
integrating GSR into usability evaluations. Furthermore, the rationale for focusing on pen injectors
has been clarified, positioning them as a representative and safety-critical case for medical device
usability research. These insights collectively underscore the relevance and potential of combining
traditional usability methods with physiological data. To contextualise and further substantiate
this approach, the following section presents a structured review of existing standards, regulatory
frameworks, and prior research, thereby anchoring the study within current scientific and industry
practices.
6
==PAGE:21==
Background
Key Takeaways for Background
• Usability Testing: Evaluates performance, errors, and satisfaction; GSR adds insight into
hidden arousal and cognitive load.
• GSR Basics: Measures physiological arousal through tonic and phasic signals; key metrics
include latency, amplitude, and recovery.
• Measurement Tools: Devices like the Shimmer3 GSR+ offer high-resolution and low-intrusion
data, ideal for usability studies.
• Use and Limits: GSR uncovers subtle stress responses, but requires careful handling of
variability and consideration of ethical concerns.
