3
Structured Literature Review
This section builds on the previous section by presenting a structured literature review that sit-
uates the study within current scientific and regulatory contexts. It outlines relevant usability
standards, explores existing work on physiological and traditional usability methods, and high-
lights recent advancements in Galvanic skin Response (GSR) technology. Finally, it examines how
the test environments influence the outcomes. Together, these insights inform the methodological
framework of this study and clarify the rationale for integrating GSR into usability evaluations.
Figure 1: PRISMA flow diagram of the literature selection process. The process involved screening,
duplicate removal, and eligibility assessment, resulting in the final selection of studies included in
the structured literature review.
The structured literature review in this study follows the PRISMA flow diagram, see Figure 1
[8]. Relevant keywords were identified based on the key areas of interest, and search strings were
formulated accordingly. The literature search was conducted across three selected search platforms,
following a structured identification process. A detailed overview of the screening process for all
8
==PAGE:23==
Structured Literature Review
literature studies can be found in Appendix A, B, C, and D.
The usability of medical devices is critical to ensuring safety and efficiency. Yet, traditional evalu-
ations often overlook physiological arousal indicators, such as GSR, which provide deeper insights
into cognitive load and user experience [41][11]. This literature review explores regulatory frame-
works, including IEC 62366-1:2020 [1] and FDA human factors guidance [18], alongside advance-
ments in GSR technology and its potential to complement traditional usability metrics, particu-
larly in medical environments where arousal can impact device interaction [5][12]. This review also
explores alternative usability testing methods and emphasises the importance of environmental
factors, as they can significantly influence arousal and decision-making [63][38].
3.1
Standards and Procedures
Medical devices have a significant impact on patient outcomes, ranging from life-threatening situ-
ations to everyday use. Given their complexity and societal importance, they are highly regulated,
especially for innovative products, which require extensive safety, usability, and benefit assessments
[65][46].
The primary goal of usability in medical devices is to enhance usability while minimising risks of
accidental misuse [24].
This literature review examines usability regulations, comparing frameworks in the U.S. and EU,
exploring classification-based differences, and outlining necessary processes, tests, and regulatory
requirements for usability in this study.
3.1.1
Standards and Procedures of Today
To ensure safety, effectiveness, and regulatory compliance, medical device development is guided
by established standards and procedures. This section reviews the most relevant regulatory frame-
works, including IEC 62366-1, IEC 62366-2, FDA Guidance, and AAMI HE75, with a focus on how
they shape usability engineering practices. Understanding these standards is crucial for aligning
this study with current expectations for integrating human factors in medical device design.
Providing evidence that these devices can be used safely has become an increasingly vital com-
ponent of pre-market applications worldwide [54]. As a result, most regulatory authorities have
9
==PAGE:24==
Structured Literature Review
established or adopted guidelines detailing how human factors engineering (HFE) should be incor-
porated into the development of new devices. Branaghan et al. list the four key HFE regulatory
documents that manufacturers should follow based on the device type and the market in which it
will be sold [54] p. 202:
• IEC 62366-1:2015/A1:2020 - Medical Devices - Application of Usability Engi-
neering to Medical Devices. Issued by the International Electrotechnical Commission
(IEC), this standard applies worldwide to both medical devices and in vitro diagnostic devices
(IVDs).
• FDA Guidance - Applying Human Factors and Usability Engineering to Medical
Devices. Issued by the U.S. Food and Drug Administration (FDA), this guidance provides
recommendations for medical devices and IVDs marketed in the US.
• FDA Draft Guidance - DRAFT: Human Factors Studies and Related Clinical
Study Considerations in Combination Product Design and Development. This
draft guidance by the FDA addresses the specific requirements for combination products and
is intended for devices that integrate drugs with devices, pending finalisation.
• MHRA Guidance - Human Factors and Usability Engineering:
Guidance for
Medical Devices Including Drug-Device Combination Products. Issued by the UK
Medicines and Healthcare products Regulatory Agency (MHRA), this document applies to
medical devices, IVDs, and combination products marketed in Great Britain.
However, Branaghan et al. do not mention another critical guidance: the AAMI HE75 - Human
Factors Engineering - Design of Medical Devices [7]. This widely used AAMI document provides
detailed guidance on applying human factors engineering to the design of medical devices. Although
it is not a formal international standard like IEC 62366-1, it has become a de facto reference,
especially in the US, for integrating usability engineering throughout device development.
Context-wise, the regulatory guidances are very similar. Looking at IEC [1] and FDA [18] in a
short gap analysis, see Appendix E, they are not far from each other. By using one of the two
guidelines, the device should generally comply.
10
==PAGE:25==
Structured Literature Review
3.1.2
Deep Dive into IEC 62366-1:2020 and IEC 62366-2:2016
As the HFE report for the regulatory submission encompasses many aspects not relevant to the
scope of this study, only some parts of it are presented here. In addition, this study will comply
with IEC 62366-1:2020 and IEC 62366-2:2016 as they apply worldwide.
The IEC 62366 standard is divided into two parts: Part 1, published in 2015 (with minor updates
in 2020), outlines the usability engineering process [1], while Part 2, released in 2016, provides
guidance on implementing it [2]. This process is designed to identify and mitigate use errors early
in the device development, thereby reducing risks associated with device usage.
According to the standard, usability engineering must consider interactions with software, trans-
portation, storage, installation, operation, maintenance, repair, and disposal. Manufacturers must
minimise use-related risks through three approaches: designing for safety, integrating protective
measures, and providing clear safety information. User manuals, warnings, and labels must un-
dergo usability testing to ensure clarity and proper use.
Clause 4 of IEC 62366-1:2020 outlines the usability engineering process, ensuring medical devices
are designed to minimise use errors and risks. It mandates a Usability Engineering File to document
all activities and results, integrating usability engineering with risk management. Being compliant
with the clause ensures a structured approach, clear documentation, and effective risk mitigation,
enhancing device safety.
Clause 5 outlines the usability engineering process, which begins with a plan that defines ac-
tivities, responsibilities, and timelines. The analysis phase identifies user needs, characteristics,
environments, and potential use errors. Usability principles are then integrated into the design and
development process. Verification ensures the design meets usability requirements, while validation
tests the device with users in realistic conditions. Implementing this clause provides a user-centred
approach, comprehensive testing, and evidence of safety and effectiveness.
By incorporating these clauses into the usability test, it ensures that the testing process is thorough
and is compliant with international standards. This will help identify and mitigate use errors more
effectively, ultimately leading to a safer and more effective device.
11
==PAGE:26==
Structured Literature Review
3.1.3
Classification of Medical Devices
Medical devices are categorised based on their potential risk to patients and users.
Both the
US and EU have established classification systems to regulate medical devices, ensuring safety,
effectiveness, and compliance with strict regulatory standards. Although both use a risk-based
approach, their classification criteria and approval processes differ.
The EU classifies medical devices under the Medical Device Regulation (MDR) 2017/745 [49].
Devices are divided into four classes based on risk level:
• Class I (Low Risk): Covers non-invasive devices like bandages and reusable surgical instru-
ments. Manufacturers self-certify compliance but must implement a Quality Management
System (QMS) in accordance with ISO 13485. Certain Class I devices (e.g., sterile or mea-
suring devices) require Notified Body (NB) involvement.
• Class IIa (Medium-Low Risk): Includes devices like hearing aids and infusion pumps. These
require an NB review for compliance.
• Class IIb (Medium-High Risk): Covers more complex devices such as ventilators and infusion
pumps delivering medication. These face stricter regulatory scrutiny and also require NB.
• Class III (High Risk): Includes devices like implantable defibrillators and drug-eluting stents.
These require clinical investigations and NB approval before receiving a CE mark for market
access.
3.1.4
Regulatory Oversight and Quality System Requirements in the EU
Beyond classification, medical devices must comply with strict quality and regulatory standards
before they can be marketed.
While both the US and EU prioritise safety and performance,
their approval processes vary.
In the EU, manufacturers must adhere to the MDR 2017/745
and implement a quality management system (QMS) based on ISO 13485:2016 [49]. For higher-
risk devices, compliance is assessed by an independent Notified Body (NB), which audits the
manufacturer’s Quality Management System (QMS) and approves the device for CE marking.
Regulating and applying usability engineering to medical devices is vital for their safety and
effectiveness in both critical and everyday use.
As technology advances, balancing innovation
12
==PAGE:27==
Structured Literature Review
with testing and compliance becomes increasingly challenging. Adhering to standards like IEC
62366-1:2020 and 62366-2:2016 helps minimise misuse risks and use errors. A strong focus on
usability, risk management, and precise documentation ensures the development of safer and more
reliable devices.
This regulatory understanding forms the basis for the usability tests in this
study, providing a thorough and globally compliant approach. For example, conducting formative
studies, the IEC 62366-2 Annex K states that small groups (typically 5–8 participants) are usually
enough to uncover major usability issues [2]. In addition, the test conductors must strive to find
participants representative of each intended user group [1].
The regulatory frameworks reviewed here provide a structured foundation for usability engineering
and highlight the importance of minimising use-related risks through iterative testing. While these
guidelines support safe and practical design, they rely heavily on observable outcomes and do not
explicitly address internal cognitive or emotional states. This gap highlights the importance of
exploring physiological measures, such as galvanic skin response (GSR), as a means to complement
existing usability evaluation protocols. To address this, recent research has explored physiological
metrics, such as GSR, eye tracking, and cognitive workload assessment, to provide objective insights
into usability. The following section examines how these alternative methods enhance traditional
usability testing.
3.2
Related Work
Ensuring the usability of medical devices is crucial for user safety, efficiency, and the overall ef-
fectiveness of the device. Traditional usability testing methods, such as task completion analysis,
heuristic evaluations, and compliance with regulatory frameworks like IEC 62366-1 [1], provide
structured approaches for usability validation. However, these methods often fail to capture cog-
nitive workload and physiological arousal, as highlighted by Longo’s study Experienced mental
workload, perception of usability, their interaction and impact on task performance, which explic-
itly notes that traditional usability evaluations overlook ’the cognitive and emotional state of the
users’ [45].
To address these limitations, this section explores alternative usability evaluation methods, includ-
ing galvanic skin response (GSR) assessments, which range from simulation-based studies and eye
tracking analyses to physiological measures such as heart rate variability (HRV), electromyography
13
==PAGE:28==
Structured Literature Review
(EMG), and electroencephalography (EEG).
3.2.1
General Approaches to Usability Testing in Medical Devices
Schmettow et al. (2017) [48] propose an extended usability validation protocol using interaction
tracing, longitudinal study design, and statistical modelling. They assess errors and inefficiencies
through normative path deviations, which are divergences from a predefined sequence of actions
for a given task. This focus on deviations from an ideal path enables a detailed analysis of user per-
formance, concentrating on metrics such as completion times, keystroke counts, and self-reported
workload [48]. Longitudinal Testing evaluates learnability, while Generalised Linear Mixed-Effects
Models identify performance trends and individual differences [48].
Wegner et al. (2020) [59] use eye tracking to classify handling tasks in a peritoneal dialysis patient
assistance device. They analyse fixation duration and dwell time to assess cognitive workload and
attention distribution, comparing two interface designs to identify usability challenges [59].
Widhalm et al.
(2023) [27] also apply eye tracking to evaluate medical device usability.
By
recording gaze behaviour, they identify hazardous or counterintuitive design elements, providing
insights for user-centred improvements [27].
Dadiz et al. (2023) [52] examine in situ simulations in neonatal care to identify safety threats
in communication, workflow, and equipment use. Unlike traditional usability testing, simulation-
based methods assess usability within the clinical system. The study highlights virtual reality
simulations as a cost-effective tool for early usability evaluation, integrating human factors and
system-wide analysis to provide real-world insights into medical devices [52].
3.2.2
Physiological Approaches in Usability Evaluation
Usability testing in medical devices evaluates task performance and human factors (HF) to ensure
efficiency and safety. Simulation-based testing assesses user interaction in realistic settings. A
study on ICU physiologic monitors measured task success, completion times, and navigation paths,
revealing inefficiencies in monitor navigation and alarm management [42]. The test highlighted
the need for a user interface (UI) redesign to improve workflow, safety, and reduce alarm fatigue
[42] [41].
14
==PAGE:29==
Structured Literature Review
Duffy and Lightner [23] highlight physiological measures as objective, real-time methods for as-
sessing arousal and cognitive load in usability studies [39].
One example is optical heart rate
monitors, which use LED sensors and photoplethysmography (PPG) to track blood flow and de-
tect arousal-related variations [23]. Common in fitness trackers, these sensors provide reasonably
accurate data, though accuracy varies based on placement [23].
They also identify respiratory rate as a key physiological indicator of arousal and cognitive load,
often correlating with HRV and blood pressure changes [23].
The study highlights the use of
surface electromyography (sEMG) for arousal detection, measuring variations in muscle activity
via electrodes placed on the biceps, triceps, forearm flexors, and trapezius muscles. sEMG offers
detailed biomechanical insights into user workload during medical device interaction [23].
Tóth presents three alternative methods to reduce use errors through usability testing. EEG is
increasingly used to measure cognitive load, offering a non-invasive method to record brain activity,
with consumer-grade devices becoming more accessible for home care and clinical testing [43]. The
Paced Auditory Serial Addition Test (PASAT) induces cognitive load, assessing attention and
information processing. In usability testing, it stimulates brain regions involved in mental effort,
enabling EEG to detect electrophysiological biomarkers of attention [43]. Lastly, respiratory rate
serves as a physiological indicator of cognitive load, with changes in breathing pattern correlating
with mental workload. Alongside HRV and blood pressure, it provides real-time insights into user
arousal during medical device interaction [43].
Seo et al. [69] review literature on wearable device design and its impact on wearability, highlighting
alternative usability testing methods as a valuable addition. They support existing research, stating
that combining subjective self-report scales with objective physiological assessments, such as HRV,
EMG, and EEG, provides a more comprehensive usability analysis [69] [41]. They also emphasise
that wearability is crucial for digital health and wearable medical devices, such as EEG, EMG, and
GSR. Comfortable and intuitive designs enhance user adherence, as assessed through ergonomic
evaluations, movement analysis, and sensor tracking. Wearability is defined by utility (necessary
features) and usability (ease and comfort of use) [69].
These physiological methods offer quantitative alternatives to standard usability evaluations, as-
sessing arousal and cognitive effort in medical device research. The studies reviewed demonstrate
15
==PAGE:30==
Structured Literature Review
that usability testing extends beyond task-based assessments, with methods such as simulations,
eye tracking, and physiological measures providing deeper insights into cognitive load, arousal, and
user interaction.
The studies reviewed in this section demonstrate a growing recognition of the limitations of tra-
ditional usability methods in capturing cognitive load and emotional arousal. By incorporating
physiological measures, such as galvanic skin response (GSR), heart rate variability (HRV), and eye
tracking, researchers have begun to address these gaps, providing a more nuanced understanding
of usability. These findings support the motivation for this study and pave the way for a closer
examination of recent advancements in GSR technology and its role in usability testing.
3.3
State of the Art
Traditional usability methods are increasingly being supplemented by physiological measures to
capture user arousal and cognitive effort, as highlighted in the previous section. Among these, gal-
vanic skin response (GSR) has emerged as a promising, non-invasive indicator of ease of use. This
section examines the current state of the art in GSR technology, with a focus on recent advance-
ments in sensor design, signal processing, and multimodal usability evaluation. Understanding
these developments is essential for assessing the feasibility and relevance of integrating GSR into
medical device usability testing.
With the increasing demand for objective physiological measures in human factors (HF) research,
GSR has gained prominence as a non-invasive method for assessing user arousal and cognitive load
[58]. Recent developments in wearable sensor technology, AI-driven analytics, and multimodal
usability evaluation frameworks have significantly enhanced the accuracy and applicability of GSR
in usability engineering [5][48]. This section explores these advancements and their implications
for medical device research.
Key technological trends shaping the evolution of GSR in usability research include:
• Enhanced Wearable GSR Sensors: Development of research-grade and consumer-level
devices improving signal accuracy, real-time wireless data collection, and long-term usability
[5][67] [58].
16
==PAGE:31==
Structured Literature Review
Figure 2: Wearable GSR sensors [67].
• AI-Powered Physiological Analysis: Machine learning models optimising signal process-
ing, improving arousal detection, and refining cognitive state classification [3][57][26].
• Integration with Multimodal Usability Testing: Combining GSR with heart rate vari-
ability (HRV), eye tracking, and facial recognition to provide deeper insights into user expe-
rience and cognitive workload [71][5][48].
These advances have significantly enhanced the precision and applicability of GSR measurements in
usability testing, making them a valuable tool for evaluating user interaction with medical devices
in both controlled and real-world settings. The following subsection will delve deeper into these
recent developments, highlighting key innovations in GSR sensor technology and signal processing.
3.3.1
Advancements in GSR Sensor Technology and Signal Processing
Modern GSR sensors have evolved from bulky laboratory setups to compact, high-precision wear-
able devices, offering seamless integration into usability research. Research-grade sensors such as
Shimmer3 GSR+ and Empatica E4 offer high sampling rates (as mentioned in Section 2) and ad-
vanced signal processing to minimise unwanted noise and distortions [58]. Additionally, consumer
devices like Fitbit Sense and Garmin Venu 2 Plus have introduced electroencephalography (EDA)
tracking, making arousal monitoring more accessible, although with limitations in signal accuracy
and research-grade usability [5][34].
Recent advances in wearable GSR technology have focused on improving sensor comfort and long-
term usability. The development of thin and flexible materials reduces discomfort associated with
traditional electrode-based sensors, enhancing wearability for extended studies in medical settings
[22]. Additionally, AI-driven signal processing has improved GSR analysis by enhancing noise
17
==PAGE:32==
Structured Literature Review
reduction and enabling real-time classification of cognitive states [25][57]. The implementation of
edge computing further supports real-time arousal analysis by allowing data processing directly
on wearable devices, reducing latency and enhancing usability in clinical environments [71].
Despite these advancements, no single commercially available GSR device integrates all these ca-
pabilities. Research-grade wearables offer high signal accuracy and real-time data transmission,
but they lack built-in AI-driven analysis or edge computing features [5][67]. Meanwhile, emerg-
ing multimodal wearable prototypes aim to combine GSR with photoplethysmography (PPG)
and electrocardiography (ECG) for enhanced cognitive state classification, although these remain
largely in research environments [26]. Future developments are expected to focus on sensor fusion
techniques and optimised on-device AI models, ultimately aiming to deliver more comprehensive
arousal and cognitive load assessment solutions for usability testing in both clinical and commercial
applications [22][3][71].
3.3.2
Advancement in Usability Testing with GSR
Traditional usability testing in medical technology primarily relies on observational studies and
retrospective user feedback to assess how users interact with medical devices [44]. While these
methods provide valuable insights, they lack real-time physiological data that can reveal hidden
cognitive load and arousal responses during device interaction. Integrating GSR into usability
testing offers a more objective and dynamic approach by capturing users’ physiological reactions,
helping to refine device design and improve user experience [54][48].
Recent developments have established GSR as a key metric in multimodal usability testing frame-
works, offering more profound insights into user interaction beyond traditional observational anal-
ysis. One significant advancement is the integration of GSR with other biometric measures, such
as HRV, eye tracking, and facial expression analysis, which together enable a more comprehensive
evaluation of user arousal and cognitive workload in medical device interactions [5][71].
Another notable development is the application of GSR in real-time usability testing within clin-
ical environments. The emergence of wireless and unobtrusive GSR sensors has facilitated their
use in hospital settings, where researchers can now measure arousal responses during high-stakes
interactions with complex medical devices, such as robotic surgical systems [3][5] and pen injectors,
for example. These advancements enhance the precision and applicability of usability testing in
18
==PAGE:33==
Structured Literature Review
real-world medical scenarios by capturing physiological responses as they occur.
Furthermore, regulatory and standardisation trends are increasingly emphasising the role of physi-
ological arousal indicators in human factors engineering (HFE). International guidelines, including
those outlined by the Food and Drug Administration (FDA) and the EU’s Medical Device Regula-
tion (MDR), now encourage the integration of biometric data into usability evaluations, reinforcing
the importance of GSR in usability research [48][54]. Collectively, these advancements highlight
GSR’s potential to enrich usability testing by providing objective physiological data that comple-
ments observational methods, ultimately improving the evaluation of medical device interactions.
While these advancements demonstrate the growing role of physiological metrics in usability test-
ing, commercially available GSR devices vary in their capabilities. The Shimmer3 GSR+, used
in this study, provides high-resolution GSR measurement but does not yet incorporate AI-driven
analysis or multimodal biometric integration [67]. However, the scope of this research offers a
reliable and research-grade foundation for integrating physiological arousal measurement into us-
ability testing workflows. Future studies may explore more advanced GSR solutions as they become
available, further enhancing the precision and applicability of biometric usability assessments.
Recent advancements in GSR technology have enhanced its integration into usability testing, al-
lowing for more precise measurement of physiological arousal responses. However, the effectiveness
of these measurements is influenced by the test environment, making it essential to consider how
test environment factors impact usability evaluation outcomes. This necessity, therefore, leads into
the next section, which explores the role of environmental factors in usability testing.
3.4
Test Environment Factors
While technological advancements, such as galvanic skin response (GSR), enhance the depth of
usability data, the context in which testing occurs remains equally important. Test environments,
constraints, and user expectations all shape the validity and reliability of usability outcomes. This
section examines how test environment factors influence user behaviour, physiological responses,
and ultimately the insights gained from usability testing, particularly in the context of medical
devices where realism and user safety are critical. The conditions in which testing occurs signif-
icantly impact user interactions, performance, and safety outcomes. Three key factors, the test
19
==PAGE:34==
Structured Literature Review
environment, constraints, and priming, must be considered to ensure the validity and reliability of
usability validation studies.
3.4.1
Test Environment
Batliner (2019) emphasises the significance of test environment factors in medical device usability
testing, particularly in complex clinical settings [51]. Usability testing must replicate real-world
conditions, as accessibility varies. Controlled settings ensure reliability and regulatory compliance,
but can overlook risks such as product selection errors, storage conditions, and user distractions
[55]. Simulating real use environments, such as home-use devices stored in refrigerators, helps assess
usability risks [63]. Factors like similar packaging, time pressure in pharmacies, and household
distractions influence error rates and must be considered for realistic usability studies [63].
The regulatory guidances stress the importance of the test environments in which combination
products are used vary significantly and can impact user interaction. Not only the physical setup
but also factors such as temperature, lighting, noise levels, ambient activity, and the presence of
similar-looking medications can influence product use and introduce potential use errors [20][7]
[1][2].
V. Gorp et al. (2012) emphasise the importance of replicating real-world settings, as ’a product’s
hardware design often serves as its first introduction to the user,’ p. 200 l. 34 [66]. The test
environment significantly impacts user performance and error rates, as emotions influence judgment
and decision-making [66]. Usability testing must reflect real-world conditions for accuracy [53],
though fully realistic setups can be costly, especially for smaller manufacturers.
Another consideration presented by Sauer et al. (2011) [38] is the crucial role of the test environ-
ment in evaluating product performance, as user characteristics, task nature, and setting influence
outcomes. Low-fidelity testing, though cost-effective, may overestimate effectiveness, as controlled
environments fail to capture real-world variability in noise, space, and social interactions [38]. Bal-
ancing realism and cost is essential; overly artificial settings may overlook factual use errors, while
controlled conditions can introduce unrealistic issues [53]. Testing mobile medical technologies is
particularly challenging [70], as real hospital settings pose risks, restrict recordings, and disrupt
workflows. Traditional usability labs often fail to replicate clinical complexity, making full-scale
hospital ward simulations necessary for balancing realism with research control [70].
20
==PAGE:35==
Structured Literature Review
3.4.2
Alternative Test Environments
Shin et al. (2023) [40] emphasise the importance of a well-structured test environment for medical
device usability, ensuring reliability and regulatory compliance. However, financial and operational
constraints make testing all scenarios impractical, while existing guidelines offer limited guidance
on usability validation [40]. As a result, alternative environmental tests may be necessary.
Alternative testing environments, such as simulated clinical settings, usability labs, and test
benches, offer structured, reproducible usability evaluations while balancing real-world complex-
ity [51] [55]. However, overly controlled settings may obscure user difficulties, while unrealistic
modifications can introduce artificial challenges [63]. Marcilly et al. (2024) [53] compared two
usability tests, one with an actress simulating an emergency and another using a manikin with
pre-recorded instructions. While high-fidelity settings better captured real-time decision-making
errors, the added complexity and cost provided limited additional insights, suggesting cost-effective
alternatives remain viable.
Ideal test environments are often impractical, as high-fidelity hospital simulations require signif-
icant resources [70].
Evaluators must prioritise prototype realism, environmental accuracy, or
psychological engagement to balance realism and cost. Virtual reality testing offers a cost-effective
alternative, simulating real-world interactions. Sauer et al. (2011) [38] stress replicating real-world
constraints in low-fidelity tests. The Food and Drug Administration (FDA) and The International
Electrotechnical Commission (IEC) also acknowledge the challenges of recreating real-use condi-
tions, recommending alternative environments when needed [20][2].
3.4.3
Constraints
Closely related to the test environment, test constraints, whether in ideal or alternative environ-
ments, significantly impact usability evaluations.
Böes et al. (2017) [55] highlight factors such as resource limitations, ethical considerations, and
balancing early experimentation with compliance-driven evaluations. Reeves et al. (2019) add
time pressure in pharmacies and home distractions as further constraints [63].
Medical device usability is critical in high-stakes clinical settings, but testing is often constrained
by accessibility, costs, and ethical considerations [51]. In hospitals, interactions between health-
21
==PAGE:36==
Structured Literature Review
care professionals, patients, and devices must be carefully managed to ensure accurate usability
evaluations within these constraints [51].
Both Norman (2013) [13] and Valgeirsdóttir et al. (2023) [14] view constraints not just as limita-
tions but as tools for shaping the right environment. Norman (2013) [13] states that Constraints
are powerful clues, limiting the set of possible actions. The thoughtful use of constraints in design
lets people readily determine the proper course of action, even in a novel situation, p.
125 ll.
4-6. Valgeirsdóttir et al. (2023) [14] expand on this, describing constraints as both enablers and
inhibitors. The key is balance; too few lead to aimlessness, while too many can stifle progress.
Constraint management involves strategically imposing, adjusting, or removing constraints, with
metacognition playing a vital role in regulating them. Recognising this difference enhances usabil-
ity and decision-making [14].
Norman (2013) [13] identifies four types of constraints:
• Physical constraints (e.g., a USB plug fitting only one way) restrict incorrect inputs.
• Cultural constraints (e.g., red lights signalling stop) align with societal norms.
• Semantic constraints (e.g., a windshield facing forward) rely on meaning for correct actions.
• Logical constraints (e.g., missing parts in reassembling a faucet) ensure structured workflows.
Understanding and managing constraints is crucial for optimising usability, as they guide actions,
enforce structure, and support intuitive design.
3.4.4
Priming
Besides constraints, priming is another factor which can play a considerable role in the test envi-
ronment. Batliner et al. (2019) highlight that medical device usability is not just about ease of use
but a critical safety concern [51]. Human factors (HF), including cognitive load, stress, arousal,
and environmental distractions, significantly impact usability testing. User priming (influenced
by prior experience, expectations, familiarity, etc.) affects performance in assessments [51][55].
Clinicians often work under pressure and time constraints, which shape their interactions with
devices. To ensure accurate usability evaluations, testing must replicate the cognitive and physical
demands of real clinical settings [51].
22
==PAGE:37==
Structured Literature Review
Reeves et al. (2019) [63] highlight priming in medical device testing, noting that prior experience,
training, and labelling shape user behaviour, as recognised in IEC 62366-2:2016 [2]. Moderator
instructions and instructions for use (IFU) must reflect real-world use, as training can reduce errors
but also introduce bias. To address this, the FDA recommends including untrained users in valida-
tion studies [63]. Priming effects must be minimised, no matter the context [66]. However, priming
cannot be eliminated, as the test environment influences behaviour [53]. Realistic scenarios foster
natural interactions, whereas simplified setups can lead to artificial responses. Users searching for
a device in real-world settings made more selection errors than when it was directly presented, and
emergency response depended on the realism of the scenario. Sauer et al. (2011) [38] noted that
laboratory settings disrupt habitual behaviour, increasing artificial compliance rates, while real-
world environments encourage routine actions, reducing engagement with on-product information.
Environmental distractions further impact user awareness and test reliability [38].
Dahl et al. (2009) [70] define psychological fidelity as crucial for realistic engagement in usabil-
ity testing. Scenario design plays a key role, with baseline paper-based workflows helping users
compare digital solutions meaningfully [70].
Ensuring realistic test environments in usability testing is essential for accurate risk assessment.
User priming, environmental realism, and test conditions all influence results. By replicating actual
use as closely as possible, usability studies enhance the safety and effectiveness of medical devices.
However, considering cost, scalability, control over the test, and other parameters, alternative test
methods also contribute, with a positive impact on usability tests.
This literature review provides the foundation for the methodological choices of this study by
outlining the regulatory context, examining current and emerging methods in usability testing,
and exploring the technological evolution of GSR in human factors (HF) research. A key finding is
that test environment conditions, from physical layout to psychological realism, have a significant
impact on user interaction and arousal. Recognising this is essential for designing studies that
produce meaningful and transferable results. Collectively, these insights reinforce the importance
of combining physiological data with conventional usability methods to evaluate user experience
in realistic, context-sensitive ways.
23
==PAGE:38==
Structured Literature Review
Key Takeaways for Structured Literature Review
• Traditional usability testing for medical devices, while vital for safety and regulatory com-
pliance, often overlooks crucial cognitive and emotional states of users.
• There’s a growing emphasis on supplementing traditional methods with physiological mea-
sures like Galvanic Skin Response (GSR), eye-tracking, heart rate variability (HRV), and
Electroencephalogram (EEG) for deeper insights into cognitive load and arousal.
• Recent advancements in GSR technology, including enhanced wearable sensors, AI-driven
analysis, and multimodal integration, are significantly improving its precision and applica-
bility in usability testing.
• The test environment (including physical setup, constraints, and user priming) critically
influences user behaviour, physiological responses, and the validity of usability outcomes.
• Achieving realistic test conditions is essential for accurate risk assessment, though balancing
realism with cost and control often requires alternative test environments such as simulated
clinical settings.
• Ultimately, combining objective physiological data with conventional usability methods in
realistic, context-sensitive ways is crucial for developing safer and more effective medical
devices.
24
==PAGE:39==
Ease of Use
4
Ease of Use
While the previous section established the scientific and regulatory context for usability evaluation,
this section focuses on the specific concept of ease of use, an essential, yet often ambiguously
defined, aspect of usability. In the context of medical devices, ease of use plays a crucial role in
enhancing user performance, ensuring safety, and promoting treatment adherence. This section
clarifies how ease of use is defined for this study and outlines the parameters and methods used to
measure it, with a particular focus on integrating galvanic skin response (GSR) as a complementary
data source.
The term ’ease of use’ roughly speaking refers to how intuitive and straightforward a device or sys-
tem is to operate. However, it is not explicitly defined or measured in medical device standards or
other regulations. A search for ’ease of use’ on the International Organization for Standardization
(ISO) online platform under ’terms and definitions’ gives no results [61].
4.1
Definition on Ease of Use
In the medical device industry, ease of use is often considered a component of the broader concept of
usability. Usability encompasses not only ease of use but also factors such as effectiveness, efficiency,
and user satisfaction. This section will define ease of use, examining some of the definitions and
the concept of usability.
62366:2007 defines usability as a user interface (UI) characteristic that ensures effectiveness, effi-
ciency, ease of learning, and user satisfaction [61]. ISO 9241-420:2011 describes it as a product’s
ability to help users achieve goals efficiently and satisfactorily within a given context [61]. Despite
different focuses, standards like ISO 15223-2:2010 (medical device symbols) and ISO 21856:2022
(assistive products) share similar definitions, emphasising effectiveness, efficiency, and ease of learn-
ing [61].
The primary usability standard in this study, IEC 62366-1, defines usability as a user interface
characteristic that facilitates use, ensuring effectiveness, efficiency, and user satisfaction in the
intended environment [1]. It further defines effectiveness as the accuracy and completeness in
achieving goals, and efficiency as the resources used relative to effectiveness [1].
25
==PAGE:40==
Ease of Use
Besides the device UI, usability also concerns the environment and the users of the device. As the
Food and Drug Administration (FDA) further explores that the intended users of a medical device
should be able to use the device without making use errors that could compromise medical care or
patient or user safety ll. 13-14 [19].
Usability and ease of use are related but not identical. This study views ease of use as part of
usability, focusing on simplicity and intuitiveness. While usability testing [1] identifies safety risks,
ease of use testing evaluates perceived user-friendliness, which also covers cognitive aspects.
The AAMI HE75 guidance does not provide a clear definition of ease of use either [7]. It describes
it as somewhat intuitive, stating that safety and ease of use are primary design considerations (p.
248 l. 3) and that ease of use enhances accuracy and effectiveness. In Section 18.2.4.4 Control
Labelling, it states that larger font sizes (up to 24 points) improve ease of use at typical viewing
distances (p. 263 l. 18-19) [7]. However, a precise definition remains absent.
The final definition for ease of use in this study project is based on both IEC 62366 and the AAMI
HE75. It is: The degree to which a device’s UI enables its intended users to safely, effectively, and
efficiently achieve their specific goals with minimal cognitive and physical effort, while requiring
minimal training.
4.2
Measure Ease of Use
How to measure ease of use can be challenging. However, this section will explore the differences
in usability and ease of use, and state precisely how to measure ease of use.
A key difference between usability and ease of use is how they are measured. Usability relies on
structured testing, such as task completion rates, error rates, and efficiency. Ease of use, however,
is based on user perception, how effortless or intuitive a device feels. Since our definition of ease
of use includes physiological, psychological, and cognitive factors, it should be measured using
galvanic skin response (GSR).
GSR measures physiological, psychological, and cognitive responses in usability testing. Physio-
logically, it increases with physical strain, such as gripping a device too tightly. Psychologically, it
tracks arousal such as stress, frustration, or anxiety, with spikes indicating heightened emotional
26
==PAGE:41==
Ease of Use
arousal. Cognitively, higher GSR responses correlate with increased workload, such as processing
complex information on a device. While GSR has limitations (see Section 2), combining it with
video observation and questionnaires enhances the reliability of usability studies, it helps pin-
point where in the process (observing, handling, or thinking) users perceive ease of use, capturing
simplicity and intuitiveness across all three dimensions.
This section provides both a definition of ease of use and a method for measuring it, which serves
as the basis for testing in this study. However, it must be underlined that ease of use focuses more
on immediate simplicity, prioritising minimal effort and a smooth learning curve. This means that
ease of use, while important, does not always consider safety; something can feel easy to use but
still cause errors if not appropriately designed. Therefore, the testing in this study should be
viewed as part of the traditional formative usability study outlined in IEC 62366-1:2020, rather
than as a standalone method.
Key Takeaways for Ease of Use
• ’Ease of use’ lacks explicit definition in medical device standards, yet is crucial for user
performance and safety.
• This study defines it as a device’s user interface enabling safe, effective, and efficient goal
achievement with minimal effort and training.
• Unlike broader usability, ’ease of use’ is measured by user perception and physiological re-
sponses like GSR, capturing physical, psychological, and cognitive strain.
• Ease of use testing complements traditional usability studies, focusing on simplicity, but
doesn’t inherently guarantee safety.
27
==PAGE:42==
Research Question and Study Rationale
5
Research Question and Study Rationale
This section builds upon insights gained from the literature review, integrating knowledge on
physiological arousal measurement, human factors (HF), and regulatory requirements. The aim
is to investigate the extent to which galvanic skin response (GSR) may enhance or complement
traditional video-based observations of user arousal. Furthermore, the study examines how dif-
ferent test environment factors influence usability testing outcomes. As discussed in Section 3.4,
traditional usability lab environments might not fully capture real-world cognitive and emotional
pressures associated with medical device use, potentially underestimating arousal-induced usability
challenges. This study, therefore, compares arousal measurements across a standard usability lab
and a more realistic simulated clinical setting, exploring whether immersive environments produce
different physiological arousal responses and usability insights.
Medical devices, especially those used under high-stress circumstances, can induce user arousal
that potentially compromises safety and performance. As previously outlined, GSR is recognised
as an objective physiological indicator of emotional and cognitive arousal related to stress [9][12].
Although GSR has been employed in HF research to complement traditional usability metrics
(such as error rates and subjective feedback), two notable gaps exist. Firstly, usability evaluations
predominantly rely on visual observations and subjective reports, which may not capture subtle
physiological arousal responses. Secondly, GSR, despite its sensitivity, has yet to become a standard
practice in regulated medical device usability testing. Thus, more empirical evidence is needed to
validate its utility and effectiveness.
Given these considerations, the primary objective of this study is to investigate the extent to
which integrating GSR into usability evaluations yields measurable and distinct insights compared
to traditional video-based methods alone.
This leads to the following neutrally stated research question and associated hypotheses:
Research Question:
To what extent does integrating galvanic skin response into the usability evaluation of pen
injectors provide measurable insights into user arousal across different test environments
compared to standard video-based observations alone?
28
==PAGE:43==
Research Question and Study Rationale
Hypotheses:
H1 (Baseline): GSR measurements can detect task-related changes in physiological arousal
during key interactions with pen injectors, such as needle attachment, priming, and simulated
injection.
Rationale: The concept of ’ease of use’ encompasses not only successful task completion but
also the cognitive and physical effort required.
As established by foundational research from
authors like Boucsein (2012) [9], GSR provides an objective, real-time measure of physiological
arousal, which serves as a proxy for this underlying workload. Before we can use GSR to compare
different conditions (H2-H4), we must first validate that it is sensitive enough to detect arousal
changes during the specific, key interactions with the pen injectors. Therefore, this hypothesis
is foundational: it aims to confirm that GSR can function as a reliable indicator of task-induced
engagement in our study’s context.
H2: The integration of GSR will detect episodes of user arousal that are not observed through
standard video-based methods alone.
Rationale: A primary limitation of traditional usability testing, as highlighted by Longo (2018) [45]
and others in the field, is its reliance on observable behaviours, which may fail to capture the user’s
internal cognitive and emotional state. This hypothesis directly addresses this methodological gap.
It proposes to quantify the extent to which objective physiological arousal, measured by GSR,
diverges from the affective reactions visible to a human factors observer. By comparing the two
data streams, we can test the core premise of this thesis: that a significant portion of usability is
physiologically expressed but not behaviourally visible, and that GSR is a necessary tool to reveal
these hidden moments of user engagement.
H3: Participants who report higher task difficulty or lower confidence on the post-task
questionnaire will show increased physiological arousal, as measured by GSR, during the
corresponding usability tasks.
Rationale: Usability evaluations often rely on a combination of objective performance metrics
and subjective user feedback. As advocated in human-computer interaction research, for exam-
ple, by Seo et al. (2021) [69], integrating physiological data offers a third, distinct layer of insight.
29
==PAGE:44==
Research Question and Study Rationale
This hypothesis examines the relationship between a user’s conscious, retrospective self-assessment
(reported difficulty and confidence) and their real-time, unconscious physiological arousal (as mea-
sured by GSR). A strong correlation would suggest that self-reports are a reliable proxy for in-
the-moment cognitive load. A weak correlation, however, would suggest that physiological arousal
and perceived difficulty are distinct phenomena, underscoring the need for a multimodal approach
to capture the usability fully.
H4: The test environments will change the level of arousal measured by the GSR and observed
behavioural responses during the usability tasks, such as needle attachment and injection.
Rationale: As emphasised in regulatory guidance and by human factors experts such as Sauer
et al. (2011) [38], the context in which a medical device is used can significantly influence user
performance and safety. A sterile laboratory setting may not elicit the same cognitive and emo-
tional pressures as a real-world environment. This hypothesis tests this principle by systematically
comparing user responses across two distinct settings: a standard usability lab and an altered en-
vironment. By analysing both physiological (GSR) and behavioural (observational) data between
these two conditions, we aim to determine whether the testing environment has a measurable im-
pact on user arousal, thereby providing empirical evidence on the importance of ecological validity
in usability evaluations.
To address the research question, the independent variable is defined as the user’s interaction with
the pen injector, while the dependent variables are the user’s physiological arousal response (mea-
sured via GSR) and behavioural stress indicators (captured by video observation). By evaluating
both visible behaviours and physiological signals of arousal, the study aims to clarify whether
GSR adds distinct value to traditional usability methods, potentially influencing future practices
in medical device usability testing.
30
==PAGE:45==
Usability Study Approach
6
Usability Study Approach
To test the relationship between galvanic skin response (GSR) and physical data, we designed
a study to investigate the role of GSR. This section outlines the practical approach taken to
investigate the role of GSR in usability testing on the pen injectors’ user interface (UI) 1 and UI2.
It describes the study design, participant selection, test environments, and methods used to collect
and analyse both physiological and behavioural data. The aim is to assess whether integrating GSR
provides additional insight into usability that may not be captured through traditional usability
evaluation methods alone.
This formative usability study integrates GSR with traditional usability methods to objectively
assess arousal responses during user interactions with pen injectors. The study’s objective is to
evaluate whether physiological arousal indicators, as measured by GSR, can provide additional
insights beyond standard usability evaluation methods, such as video-based observations and sub-
jective questionnaires.
A total of four tests will be conducted, see Figure 3.
Figure 3: Summary of all the tests conducted.
• Pilot test 1 will be conducted on us at DTU to assess whether we get valuable GSR data.
• Pilot test 2 will be conducted at the Usability Lab at Technolution, where the final tests will
be conducted as well. The test will be conducted on two test participants from Technolution.
31
==PAGE:46==
Usability Study Approach
• Pilot test 3 will be conducted at DTU to address the inconsistent data we got from pilot test
2. We will be the test participants.
• The formative test will be conducted at Technolution’s Usability Lab with 12 test partici-
pants. Participants for the study will be recruited via convenience sampling. The sample
comprised individuals with varying familiarity with injector technology: some participants
had extensive professional experience in designing and testing pen injectors, while one par-
ticipant had once used pen injectors for personal medical purposes. Despite their varied
experience with pen injectors, all participants interacted with the same type of pen injector
during the test, ensuring consistency in device evaluation.
The study employed a within-subject experimental design. Each participant tested two distinct
pen injectors in a randomised order, mitigating potential order effects. Tests will be conducted in
two environments: the standard usability room used at Technolution A/S and the same room with
an alternative environment setting (including homelike elements). Comparing these environments
allowed the assessment of context-dependent variations in physiological arousal responses.
Data collection methods included physiological measurements using the Shimmer3 GSR+ and be-
havioural observations through video recordings. Participants completed structured questionnaires
after the interaction to capture their subjective assessments of usability, ease of use, and perceived
arousal levels during the tasks.
The analysis involved synchronising GSR data with corresponding video recordings to compare
observable behaviours with physiological responses directly. Moments of interaction that prompted
visible difficulty, hesitation, or errors will be annotated and analysed to ascertain whether the GSR
data provided additional, valuable insights into usability that traditional observational methods
alone might not reveal.
This integrated approach enables a comprehensive assessment of user arousal, facilitating the
identification of design elements and procedural factors that influence ease of use and device
usability in medical contexts.
32
==PAGE:47==
Usability Study Approach
6.1
Pilot Tests
Before conducting the actual formative tests, three individual pilot tests were carried out to de-
termine whether any adjustments to our setup were necessary, see Figure 3.
6.1.1
Pilot Test 1
To ensure the methodological robustness of integrating GSR into usability testing, a foundational
pilot test was conducted. The aim was to evaluate whether GSR data could be reliably captured,
processed, and meaningfully aligned with observed user behaviour. Additionally, this phase helped
establish a synchronisation workflow for combining physiological signals with video recordings.
Pilot Test 1 was conducted at DTU with four main objectives. Firstly, to test the GSR sensor to
determine whether it could effectively capture measurable data. Secondly, to process the GSR data
and convert it into interpretable plots that would be suitable for further analysis. Thirdly, to ex-
plore methods for aligning the GSR output with video recordings to enhance data synchronisation.
Finally, as the GSR device also includes a PPG sensor for measuring pulse, an additional objective
was to assess its potential impact and determine whether it could provide valuable insights for the
study. In total, six tests were carried out.
The first step of our pilot test was to develop and refine a workflow for synchronising GSR data with
video recordings. This workflow was essential for enabling a comprehensive analysis of physiological
responses alongside observed user behaviours. The process we developed consists of the following
steps:
• Trimming Baseline: To ensure that the GSR data and video recordings were aligned, the
baseline period was removed.
• Extracting Key Frames: Frames were extracted from the videos; this provided visual
reference points for moments of heightened physiological response.
• Overlaying Frames with GSR Graphs: To enhance interpretability, the video frames
were displayed beneath the corresponding GSR signal graph. This ensured that fluctuations
in skin conductance could be directly compared to user actions and contextual events in the
video footage.
33
==PAGE:48==
Usability Study Approach
• Annotating Arousal Events: Annotations were added to mark important moments in
the video and GSR timeline. These annotations included timestamps for arousal reactions,
observed difficulties, and contextual factors that could explain physiological variations.
To validate this workflow, an early, informal test was conducted using a known physiological
stimulus (consuming spicy food) to confirm the sensor’s basic sensitivity. A detailed analysis of
one such test is presented in Table 1, which correlates five distinct GSR peaks with the participant’s
observable reactions and verbal comments from the video recording.
Table 1: Example of annotated GSR peaks from Pilot Test 1.
GSR Peak Timestamp
Corresponding Video Reaction
03:08
Closes eyes nervously, not moving hands,
smiles nervously.
03:37
Not moving hand.
Eating chilli, talking
about the chilli being a little spicy and dis-
likes the taste.
04:29
Moving hands (possibly noise), eating chilli,
saying it is a little spicy.
05:16
Not moving, no reaction, saying the chilli
doesn’t taste like much.
05:45
Not moving, opening eyes and talking while
eating chilli.
This initial analysis confirmed that the GSR sensor could reliably capture and visualise usable
physiological data. The example in Table 1 demonstrates this by showing that the recorded peaks
were not random noise but were directly linked to specific events. This exercise was foundational
for three key reasons:
1. It validated the sensor’s sensitivity: The device successfully captured clear physiological
responses to both anticipatory stress (the nervous reaction at 03:08) and the direct physical
stimulus (eating the chilli).
2. It revealed methodological challenges: The analysis immediately highlighted the need
to account for potential movement artefacts (as noted at 04:29), reinforcing the importance
of combining GSR with careful video observation to interpret the data correctly.
3. It uncovered a core theme for the main study: Most importantly, it provided the first
evidence of a disconnect between subjective reports and physiological arousal. The peak at
34
==PAGE:49==
Usability Study Approach
05:16, where the participant verbally downplayed the experience (’does not taste like much’),
proved that the sensor could capture internal reactions that the user does not fully express.
In addition, Pilot Test 1 demonstrated that handling an achillii, shaped similarly to a pen injector,
with one’s hands does not produce a GSR peak.
The findings from the first pilot test laid the foundation for Pilot Study 2, ensuring that both GSR
and video data could be effectively integrated into the analysis pipeline.
6.1.2
Pilot Test 2
Following the first pilot test, a Pilot Test 2 was conducted to test the protocol of the formative
study, the test environment, the GSR system, the code, and the selected pen injectors.
The
objective was to ensure that all components functioned as intended, allowing for a smooth and
effective execution of the actual formative usability test.
Figure 4: Pilot Participant 1 injection using UI2.
The Pilot Test 2 followed the structured protocol outlined in the Formative Usability Test Protocol,
see Appendix F, and was conducted in a controlled environment at Technolution’s Usability Lab.
Key aspects, including test session structure, video recording, GSR data collection, and moderator
instructions, were evaluated to assess their feasibility and impact on the study’s overall execution.
The Pilot Test 2 was conducted with two participants.
The participants were asked to complete the following tasks, where subtasks 1.1 to 1.5 constitute

Adjustments and Learnings from Pilot Test 2:
• Environmental Distractions: It was observed that the transparent door and walls in
the test environment introduced distractions that could potentially impact the participants’
focus. To mitigate this, curtains will be drawn during test sessions to create a controlled and
distraction-free environment.
• Synchronous Data Collection for GSR and Video Recording: Initially, the moder-
ator had to start the video and GSR recording separately, causing disruption and diverting
attention. To address this, a co-moderator was added to the protocol to manage simultaneous
recording, streamlining the process and minimising interruptions.
36
==PAGE:51==
Usability Study Approach
• Instruction for Checking Drug Flow: The pilot participant was unsure how to check
drug flow before injection. A brief instruction sheet was added to the protocol to clarify the
procedure without affecting the overall usability assessment.
• Room Temperature Adjustments: Although the room temperature was set at 20°C,
the pilot participant reported feeling too cold. To create a more comfortable and home-like
environment for participants, the heating will be adjusted to a slightly warmer temperature
in future sessions.
• Stronger Scenario Contextualisation: As participants were not actual pen injector users,
it became evident that a clearer scenario was needed. An enhanced scenario description was
added to the moderator script to improve task engagement and help participants better
understand the context and purpose of their actions.
• Ethical Considerations for Attaching GSR and Injection Pad: The protocol was
revised to ensure that the moderator seeks explicit consent before attaching the GSR device
and injection pad. Participants may choose to attach them themselves or have the moderator
assist them.
• Safe Placement of Devices: After attaching the GSR device and injection pad, the mod-
erator should check that the fit is comfortable and secure. This ensures participant comfort
and that the injection pad remains in place during use.
• Recorded Skin Conductance: The data from the GSR begins when pressing ’start to
PC’ on the Consensys software and not when pressing the play button as originally thought.
• Refinement of Data Analysis Code: The pilot test data prompted adjustments to the
analysis code, including the separation of tonic and phasic GSR components.
Crucially,
it highlighted the need to establish a clear, evidence-based threshold for peak detection, a
process which was formalised in the final analytical method (see Section 6.3.3).
These refinements were incorporated into the final version of the protocol to enhance study re-
liability and ensure that usability issues are assessed under optimal conditions. By addressing
these issues early in the pilot test process, the study design was strengthened, reducing potential
confounding variables and improving the validity of the collected data.
37
==PAGE:52==
Usability Study Approach
6.1.3
Pilot Test 3
During the Pilot Test 2, unexpected discrepancies were observed between GSR signals and user
behaviour. This prompted a closer investigation into potential causes, such as individual phys-
iological variation, sensor placement issues, and the timing of specific tasks. Addressing these
inconsistencies was essential to ensure that the physiological data would be both interpretable and
relevant in the formative usability study.
One possible explanation was that we had missed an update for the Consensys software; however,
this was ruled out. Another consideration was the PPG measurement. In Pilot Test 1, we found
that PPG did not provide usable data, so we chose not to include it in the pilot tests. The software
interface allows users to toggle measurement options on and off, raising the question of whether
we had neglected to disable the PPG option, which could have influenced the data.
Further potential issues were related to electrode placement and participant-specific factors. On
the first participant in the second pilot test, the electrodes were not secured tightly enough, causing
them to shift on the participant’s fingers. This issue was addressed in the second participant for the
second pilot test, where the electrodes were properly fastened and remained in place. Additionally,
the effectiveness of the electrodes can depend on the presence of hair on the fingers or their
positioning. While they are typically placed on the front of the fingers, an alternative placement
on the back of the fingers could have influenced the data. However, it was also important to
consider whether this placement would interfere with finger movement and the use of the pen
injector.
Another potential source of interference was jewellery. Both participants in the Pilot Test 2 were
wearing rings, which might have affected the electrodes and introduced white noise. Additionally,
the use of hand lotion by participants could have altered skin conductivity, meaning that cleaning
the skin with alcohol wipes beforehand may be necessary.
Addressing these inconsistencies, a Pilot Test 3 was conducted:
• Mitigation 1: Hanna, with a ring on her finger, tried with both PPG and GSR data chosen,
the electrodes positioned on the front of the fingers.
The data was recorded accurately
without any issues.
38
==PAGE:53==
Usability Study Approach
• Mitigation 2: Hanna, with ring on her finger, only with GSR data chosen (no PPG), the
electrodes positioned on the front of the fingers. The data was recorded accurately without
any issues.
• Mitigation 3: Hanna with only GSR data chosen (no PPG), electrodes positioned on the
back of the fingers. The data was recorded accurately without any issues.
• Mitigation 4: Hanna with both PPG and GSR data chosen, electrodes positioned on the
back of the fingers. The data was recorded accurately without any issues.
• Mitigation 5: Hanna wearing the device for 15 minutes with only GSR data chosen (no
PPG), electrodes positioned on the front of the fingers. The data was recorded accurately
without any issues.
• Mitigation 6: Tara wearing the device for 15 minutes only with GSR data chosen (no PPG),
electrodes positioned on the front of the fingers. The data was recorded accurately without
any issues.
Despite these investigations, we were unable to determine the exact cause of the discrepancies,
as the Pilot Test 3 produced correct data. The issue may have been due to improper electrode
fastening or an incorrect selection of PPG instead of GSR.
Since the data was recorded smoothly, we proceeded with the formative usability study while
closely monitoring live data during the baseline questions to ensure accuracy. However, we added
the use of alcohol wipes on the participants’ fingers as an extra hygienic precaution.
This investigation highlighted the importance of contextualising physiological data within the
broader test scenario. By identifying sources of variability early on, the study was able to refine
its methodological approach, improving the accuracy and consistency of GSR interpretation in the
main usability evaluation.
This section outlines the entire methodological progression leading up to the formative usability
study. Pilot Test 1 confirmed the technical feasibility of using GSR in combination with video
recordings and provided a framework for synchronising and annotating physiological data. Pilot
Test 2 then allowed for refinement of both procedural elements and data processing techniques,
helping to identify initial usability challenges and ensuring that the setup captured meaningful vari-
ations in user arousal. Lastly, the analysis of discrepancies in Pilot Test 3 informed adjustments to
39
==PAGE:54==
Usability Study Approach
the experimental design, reinforcing the importance of careful calibration and contextual interpre-
tation of physiological signals. Together, these preparatory steps established a robust foundation
for the formative usability study, enabling a well-integrated evaluation of usability and arousal.
Together, the three pilot tests established a strong methodological foundation for the main us-
ability evaluation. They enabled the team to refine technical procedures, ensure reliable GSR
data collection, and optimise the test setup for ecological validity and participant comfort. With
these refinements in place, the study proceeded to the final phase: a formative usability test de-
signed to systematically evaluate user interactions with pen injectors under varying environmental
conditions using both traditional and biometric measures.
6.2
Formative Test Method
This section outlines the procedure that will be followed during the main usability study, a forma-
tive usability test, which will take place in Technolution’s usability lab.
The usability study will follow a detailed formative study protocol. This protocol outlines all
procedural elements, from participant instructions to task sequencing and timing, and serves as the
operational backbone of the study. For full details on the protocol, see Appendix F. This protocol
was tested and validated during Pilot Test 2 (see Section 6.1.2) to ensure that all procedures, from
moderator instructions to task sequencing and data recording, are clearly defined and executable.
The protocol ensures consistency across sessions and allows for reliable replication of the test.
The formative test will explore how different environmental conditions affect physiological arousal,
and whether GSR can add an extra dimension to usability testing in MedTech, specifically by
identifying signs of user arousal that may not be visible through video observation alone. The study
will be conducted in two controlled environments within the same usability lab: one representing a
standard environment and the other designed to simulate a more domestic setting by introducing
visual and auditory cues. The test environment will be described further in Section 6.2.1. An
overview of the formative usability test design is summarised in Figure 5.
40
==PAGE:55==
Usability Study Approach
Figure 5: Overview of the order and process of the formative usability test.
The formative usability study will be applied to explore how users interact with two different
pen injector devices. This type of study is used during the early stages of product development
to identify potential usability issues before final design decisions are made [1] [2]. It allows for
iterative feedback and design refinement based on real user interaction, rather than relying solely
on theoretical or expert evaluation.
Participants will be asked to complete two simulated use scenarios: attaching a needle to a pen
injector and delivering a simulated injection using a diabetes injection pad. They will complete the
two use scenarios while wearing a GSR sensor, which measures their physiological arousal responses.
In addition to physiological data, the study will gather user feedback through observation, verbal
comments, and post-task questionnaires.
To ensure meaningful integration of biometric and behavioural data, the GSR device and video
recordings were initiated simultaneously. Before the official tasks began, a baseline measurement
period of a few minutes was always observed. This crucial step allowed participants to acclimate to
the sensor and the test environment. During this time, it was common to observe an initially high
GSR signal that would gradually decrease and stabilise as the participant became calmer. Once
this steady state was reached, the formal tasks commenced. Any minor second variations in record-
ing start times were carefully documented to allow for precise correlation between physiological
responses (GSR) and observable behaviours (video) during the subsequent analysis.
By following the protocol, the method will involve structured task execution under observation,
with moderators noting behaviours, errors, and any signs of confusion. This will also be supported
by video recording, real-time GSR data collection, and structured subjective assessments to gain
41
==PAGE:56==
Usability Study Approach
a comprehensive understanding of usability performance.
A moderator script is a crucial component of the protocol. It is used to ensure all participants
receive identical instructions and phrasing throughout the study. This standardisation helps reduce
bias and maintain consistency across sessions. The moderator should follow the script and only
deviate in cases where a participant risks injury or commits potentially hazardous usability use
errors or close calls.
This approach is instrumental in medical device design, as it enables researchers to discover design
weaknesses, user misunderstandings, and contextual influences before formal validation testing [1].
By adhering to a structured and comprehensive protocol, the study minimises variability across
sessions and supports the generation of reliable and comparable data. This consistency is critical
for both the usability evaluation and the accurate interpretation of GSR data across participants
and environments.
6.2.1
Staging the Test Environment
The test environment plays a crucial role in shaping user behaviour and physiological responses (see
Section 3.4.1). This section describes how the study environments were staged to reflect varying
levels of realism and contextual fidelity, enabling the exploration of environmental influence on
both usability and GSR readings.
The design of the test environment in this formative usability study shows an apparent effort
to reflect the key concerns raised in the literature around test environment factors. Across both
the practical setup and conceptual framing, the study closely aligns with scientific and regulatory
guidance on how usability testing environments can impact user interaction, error rates, and the
overall validity of findings.
The literature strongly emphasises the importance of replicating real-world conditions in medical
device testing.
Authors such as Batliner and Sauer argue that while controlled environments
provide consistency and compliance with regulatory standards, they may overlook critical factors,
including distractions, temperature variations, or lighting conditions, that commonly affect device
use in everyday settings. The study responded to this by creating two contrasting environments:
one reflecting a standard usability lab (Environment A), and another adapted to mimic a more
42
==PAGE:57==
Usability Study Approach
domestic setting (Environment B), featuring plants and background noise calibrated at 55 dB, see
Figure 6 and 7.
(a) Test Environment A (entrance view).
(b) Test Environment B (entrance view).
Figure 6: Entrance views of the two environments.
Both environments were situated in the same physical room at Technolution, ensuring structural
consistency while allowing key environmental variables to be adjusted. This approach achieves
a balance between control and realism.
In doing so, the study aligns with the principle that
overly controlled settings can underestimate usability issues, while entirely realistic ones may be
logistically impractical or inconsistent.

Usability Study Approach

The test setup also carefully incorporated constraints as part of the test environment’s staging.
The layout of the room, positioning of the equipment, and a marked area on the table for simulating
the injection were all standardised to reduce variability across sessions. Constraints such as these
serve not only to structure tasks but also to reflect the types of decisions users must make under
44
==PAGE:59==
Usability Study Approach
real-world conditions.
These constraints were not overly prescriptive, allowing participants to
approach each task with a degree of freedom, while still maintaining the structure necessary to
produce reliable data.
Priming, another key factor discussed in the literature, was also taken into consideration. The
study design aimed to minimise the influence of participant expectations or prior experience by
avoiding unnecessary instruction, randomising the order in which pen injectors were tested, and
using moderators to deliver standardised scripts.
In summary, the staging of the test environment of this formative usability study closely mirrors
best practices and current thinking within the field. By integrating environmental realism with a
controlled and repeatable structure, and by recognising the influence of cognitive and emotional
factors through thoughtful priming and constraint design, the test environment not only supports
valid and insightful findings but also strengthens the study’s methodological credibility.
The
deliberate contrast between the two test environments allows for analysis of how the setting affects
physiological arousal. By incorporating both controlled and realistic conditions, the study captures
a broader spectrum of user responses, thereby enhancing the external validity of its findings.
Key Takeaways for Usability Study Approach
• The study investigates if Galvanic Skin Response (GSR) provides additional insights into
usability with pen injectors beyond traditional usability methods.
• It employs a within-subject formative usability study design, testing two pen injectors in
varied environments (standard vs. ’homelike’).
• Data collection involves physiological measurements (Shimmer3 GSR+), video observations,
and subjective questionnaires.
• Pilot tests were crucial for refining the methodology, ensuring reliable GSR data capture,
synchronisation with video, and addressing environmental or sensor-related issues.
• The approach emphasises integrating physiological data to enhance the understanding of
ease of use and medical device usability.
