6.3
Data Analysis Methods of Formative Usability Study
With the study design and data collection procedures established, this section presents the ana-
lytical framework that will be used to interpret the multimodal data for the formative usability
study. It outlines the steps that will be taken to ensure data validity and reliability, describes how
GSR signals will be processed and analysed, and explains how user affect will be coded using video
observations based on the Self-Assessment Manikin (SAM) and circumplex model.
To ensure objectivity throughout this entire process, the analysis will be carefully sequenced to
prevent confirmation bias. We will first analyse the video recordings independently, focusing only
on observable behaviours and human factors principles, without any knowledge of the correspond-
ing GSR output. This will allow for an unbiased, observation-driven foundation. After this initial
evaluation is complete, we will integrate the supplementary data streams, such as SAM ratings,
GSR peaks, and environmental variables, to enrich and validate the usability findings.
Figure 8 outlines the planned sequence for data analysis. The workflow will begin with video
analysis, followed by behavioural coding using the SAM and circumplex models. Subsequent steps
include GSR peak detection, questionnaire analysis, and a confusion matrix evaluation, concluding
with validation through semi-structured interviews..
Figure 8: Planned sequence for data analysis.
The following sections will provide in-depth coverage of the different methods of analysis.
46
==PAGE:61==
Usability Study Approach
6.3.1
Video Analysis
First of all, a part of the ordinary way of analysing a formative study from a human factors (HF)
view will be applied. The videos will be carefully looked through at the task level. Timestamps
will be registered for any difficulties, use errors, close calls, etc, see Appendix P. At the timestamp,
a description of the reaction will be noted. The following section will look into how to analyse the
behavioural responses found in the video analysis.
6.3.2
Behavioural Analysis using SAM and the Circumplex Model
To complement the physiological data from GSR, a systematic behavioural analysis will be con-
ducted on the video recordings to code participants’ affective states during the tasks. This method
will allow for a more nuanced understanding of the usability by capturing emotional valence, which
GSR alone cannot distinguish. The analytical framework for this process will be built on two estab-
lished psychological models: the Self-Assessment Manikin (SAM) [47] and the Circumplex Model
of Affect [37].
SAM will be used to rate two fundamental dimensions of emotion on a 9-point scale:
• Valence: The degree of pleasantness, ranging from negative (a score of 1-3) to neutral (4-6)
to positive (7-9).
• Arousal: The level of intensity or activation, ranging from calm/relaxed (a score of 1-3) to
moderately activated (4-6) to highly activated/excited (7-9).
These two scores will then be used as coordinates to map the observed emotion onto the Circumplex
Model of Affect. This model categorises emotions based on their valence and arousal levels, allowing
for classifications such as ’anxious’ (high arousal and negative valence) or ’relaxed’ (low arousal
and positive valence).
Analysis Procedure: For each participant, the video recordings will be meticulously analysed
task-by-task. We will both code participant affect by observing facial expressions, posture, ges-
tures, breathing patterns, and verbal cues. Using the SAM pictorial scales as a reference, a score for
both valence and arousal will be assigned for each task. These ratings, along with qualitative notes
and the corresponding Circumplex category, will be systematically documented and recorded. To
ensure consistency, inter-rater reliability will be assessed during the coding process. The resulting
47
==PAGE:62==
Usability Study Approach
data will then be visualised using boxplots and line graphs to compare emotional responses across
different user interfaces and environmental conditions.
This structured application of the SAM framework and Circumplex Model will provide a robust
method for interpreting and quantifying observable affective user behaviour.
6.3.3
Analysis of GSR Data
To analyse physiological arousal during the usability study, raw GSR data will be processed using
Python in Google Colab. This section outlines the steps for preparing, cleaning, and interpreting
GSR data, as well as how synchronisation with behavioural video observations will be achieved.
Data Import and Preparation: Real-time physiological data from the Shimmer3 GSR+ will
be acquired using the manufacturer’s proprietary software, Consensys, which will also be used to
export the raw data as CSV files for subsequent analysis. Each file includes timestamped values
for skin conductance (in microsiemens) and resistance (in kiloohms). These files will be imported
into Google Colab using Python, where only data related to time, skin conductance, and resistance
will be retained.
Baseline Trimming and Smoothing: The initial portion of every recording, captured before
any user interaction, will be dedicated to establishing a stable physiological baseline. A stable
baseline is essential for valid GSR analysis for two primary reasons.
Firstly, it provides a reliable reference point. GSR data consists of a slow-moving tonic signal (the
baseline Skin Conductance Level, or SCL) and rapid, short-lasting phasic peaks in response to
stimuli (Skin Conductance Responses, or SCRs). To accurately identify these phasic peaks, they
must be measured against a steady tonic background. Suppose the baseline itself is fluctuating
wildly due to a participant’s initial nervousness. In that case, it becomes impossible to distinguish
an actual task-related peak from the noise of their arousal level simply settling down.
Secondly, as noted in the protocol, this period allows the participant to acclimate psychologically
to the test setting. Once a stable baseline is observed in the live data, this pre-task data will be
trimmed from the recording, ensuring that the subsequent analysis focuses solely on arousal events
that occurred during the user’s interaction with the devices. Following trimming, a smoothing
filter will be applied to reduce minor signal noise and enhance the clarity of the phasic peaks.
48
==PAGE:63==
Usability Study Approach
Tonic and Phasic Separation: To differentiate between baseline trends and stimulus-evoked
responses, the skin conductance data will be decomposed into tonic and phasic components. A
low-pass Butterworth filter will be applied to extract the tonic component (SCL). The phasic
component (SCR) will then be calculated by subtracting the tonic signal from the raw data. This
separation is necessary to isolate fast, event-related peaks associated with user reactions.
Peak Detection and Thresholding: Peaks in the phasic signal (SCR) will be identified using the
find_peaks function from the SciPy library. A critical parameter in this process is the minimum
peak height threshold, which determines what qualifies as a significant arousal event.
To select an appropriate and robust threshold, an initial exploratory analysis will be conducted on
the pilot data. This analysis will compare several different peak height values (e.g., 0.01µS, 0.05µS,
and 0.1µS) to determine an optimal balance. The goal is to identify a threshold that is sensitive
enough to capture subtle, contextually relevant arousal events visible in video recordings, while
simultaneously being high enough to filter out excessive background noise and movement artefacts.
The threshold that best meets these criteria will then be selected and applied consistently for the
primary data analysis
Synchronisation of GSR and Video Data: To allow for precise comparison between physio-
logical and behavioural responses, GSR recordings and video data will be initiated simultaneously.
While minor timing discrepancies may occur, these will be documented and adjusted for during
analysis. Each detected GSR peak will be time-stamped and aligned with previously annotated
behavioural observations from the video recordings.
Comparison with Video Observations: Detected GSR peaks will be compared with be-
havioural events identified through video analysis to evaluate whether physiological responses
align with visible user behaviours. This comparison will help determine whether GSR can re-
veal otherwise hidden moments of user discomfort that might not be captured through observation
alone.
This analytical process will enable a structured approach to interpreting physiological arousal data
from the GSR. The method is designed to uncover insights that enhance the validity of usability
testing in a medical technology (MedTech) context.
Another component of the formative usability study will involve collecting self-reported feedback
via questionnaires. The following section presents an analysis method of the questionnaire data.
49
==PAGE:64==
Usability Study Approach
6.3.4
Questionnaire Data
The questionnaire data will be analysed to capture users’ subjective experiences with the device,
including perceived ease of use, stress levels, and task difficulty.
Responses will be visualised
using bar charts to illustrate categorical distributions across environments and tasks. Box plots
will also be applied to depict variations in responses and identify potential outliers or patterns
in perceived usability, enabling comparative analysis between test settings. While the galvanic
skin response (GSR) and video data will enable us to extract external information about their
experience, the questionnaire data will provide valuable insight into the perceived knowledge of
the user. To examine the objectivity and reliability of the physiological data, a classification will
also be performed using a confusion matrix analysis.
6.3.5
Confusion Matrix
To formally and quantitatively assess the degree of alignment between the two data streams (GSR
and video observation), a classification analysis will be performed to calculate the metrics of
precision and sensitivity [6]. For this analysis, the reactions coded from the video will be treated
as the ground truth, the observable events that the physiological data aims to detect.
Based on this, each event will be classified into one of three possible outcomes:
• True Positive (TP): A GSR peak is correctly matched with a video-coded reaction.
• False Positive (FP): A GSR peak occurs without any corresponding behavioural reaction
observed in the video. This represents a physiological reaction that was not visibly expressed.
• False Negative (FN): A reaction is observed in the video, but no corresponding GSR peak
is recorded. This represents a visible user action that did not elicit a measurable physiological
response.
From these values, two key performance metrics will be calculated:
• Precision answers the question: ’Of all the times the GSR sensor detected a peak, what
proportion were correct (i.e., matched a real video event)?’
• Sensitivity (also known as Recall) answers the question: ’Of all the real video events that
happened, what proportion did the GSR sensor successfully detect?’
50
==PAGE:65==
Usability Study Approach
These will be computed using the following standard formulas:
Precision =
TP
TP + FP
(1)
Sensitivity =
TP
TP + FN
(2)
To complement the technical evaluation of GSR data accuracy, the findings will subsequently be
discussed with industry experts through semi-structured interviews, allowing for external validation
and professional reflection on the methodology and results.
6.3.6
Statistical Methods
To formally test Hypotheses 3 and 4, a series of inferential statistical tests will be performed. The
nature of the data collected will dictate the choice of tests. Because the data is either categorical
(for H3) or consists of ordinal scores and counts that are not assumed to be normally distributed
(for H4), non-parametric tests, which do not rely on the assumption of a normal distribution,
will be selected as the most appropriate and robust approach [56]. Unlike parametric tests (e.g.,
t-tests), non-parametric methods do not require the data to follow a normal distribution, making
them ideal for this study’s data.
For Hypothesis 3, which classifies each data point into one of two distinct categories (’Supports’ or
’Contradicts’), the Binomial Test will be used. This test is specifically designed to assess whether
the observed proportion of two outcomes is statistically different from a hypothesised probability,
in this case, a 50/50 random chance [56].
For Hypothesis 4, which compares the outcomes between two independent groups (Environment A
and Environment B), the Mann-Whitney U Test will be used. This is a powerful non-parametric
test used to determine whether the distribution of scores in one group is statistically different from
that in another. It will be applied to compare the ordinal questionnaire scores, the SAM scores for
Arousal and Valence, and the per-participant counts of GSR peaks and video reactions between
the two environments [56].
For all statistical tests, the outcome is a p-value. In this context, the null hypothesis is the ’no
51
==PAGE:66==
Usability Study Approach
effect’ scenario (e.g., that there is no real relationship or no difference between groups). A result is
considered statistically significant if the calculated p-value is below the conventional threshold of
p < 0.05 [4][10]. A p-value this low indicates there is less than a 5% probability that the observed
result is a random fluke, allowing us to reject the null hypothesis.
6.3.7
Semi Structured Interviews
To validate findings, semi-structured interviews will be conducted with a Senior Human Factors
Specialist and a Senior Quality Assurance Specialist. The interviews aim to gather expert in-
sights on integrating physiological data into usability evaluations. Key themes will be extracted
and compared to study results, ensuring alignment with industry expectations and identifying
opportunities for future methodological refinement.
This section has presented the complete analytical approach used to process and interpret the
formative usability study, including GSR data, behavioural observations, and contextual variables.
By combining a structured protocol with synchronised, multi-source data analysis, the study is
well-positioned to explore subtle patterns in user arousal and usability challenges.
Key Takeaways for Data Analysis Methods of Formative Usability Study
• The study employs a sequential analytical framework, starting with video analysis, then
using SAM and the Circumplex Model for emotional categorisation, followed by GSR peak
analysis, questionnaire review, and validation via interviews.
• User affect is coded from video observations using the Self-Assessment Manikin (SAM) for
valence and arousal, and the Circumplex Model for emotional states.
• Raw GSR data is processed to separate tonic and phasic components, with a threshold
applied to identify peaks in skin conductance.
• Video analysis for behavioural observation (difficulties, errors, close calls) is conducted inde-
pendently, without initial knowledge of GSR data, to avoid bias.
• The analysis prioritises the interpretability of GSR signals, as reliable physiological arousal
data is foundational to the study’s hypotheses.

Results and Analysis
This section presents the data and findings from the formative usability study. The following

Figure 9: Visual overview of the analytical flow in the ’Results and Analysis’ section. The structure

hypotheses rather than the order in which the analytical methods were described.
This is a
deliberate choice because the study’s foundation rests on the validity of the physiological data

valid.

Following the initial data processing, the galvanic skin response (GSR) time-series data for each

were then time-aligned with the video recordings to determine which user tasks corresponded with

moments of elevated physiological arousal. A complete overview of all identified GSR peaks and
their corresponding video-observed reactions for every participant is provided in Appendix P.

7.3
The Discrepancy Between Physiological and Observational Data
This section systematically investigates the relationship between the physiological data stream
(GSR) and the observational data captured on video, answering Hypothesis 2. To gain a compre-
hensive understanding of how these two methods align and diverge, the following analysis examines
the discrepancy from multiple perspectives. The investigation begins by quantifying the mismatch,
proceeds to qualitative examples for illustration, and then formally assesses the degree of align-
ment using standard performance metrics. Finally, the nature of the discrepancies themselves,
along with their relationship to observer-rated emotional states, is explored to provide a richer
context for the findings.
55
==PAGE:70==
Results and Analysis
7.3.1
Calculating Precision and Sensitivity
To formally assess the degree of alignment between the physiological (GSR) and behavioural (video)
data streams, the metrics of precision and sensitivity were calculated for each participant session,
following the methodology detailed in Section 6.3.5.
The precision and sensitivity scores for every session are visualised in Figure 12. A review of this
data reveals four key findings about the overall performance and variability of GSR as an event
detector in this study:
Figure 12: Overview of precision and sensitivity scores for all participants across both UIs.
• Overall Low Alignment: The mean scores were consistently low.
For UI1, the mean
precision was 0.24 and the mean sensitivity was 0.41. For UI2, the results were even lower,
with a mean precision of 0.18 and a mean sensitivity of 0.31. This indicates that, on average,
the two data streams did not align well.
• High Individual Variability: The scores varied dramatically between participants, ranging
from 0.0 to 0.86 for precision and 0.0 to 1.0 for sensitivity. This shows that the level of
alignment is highly dependent on the individual user.
• Frequent Lack of Overlap: A total of six participant sessions recorded a precision and
sensitivity of 0, meaning that for those entire sessions, there was not a single instance where
56
==PAGE:71==
Results and Analysis
a GSR peak and a video-observed reaction occurred together.
• Sensitivity vs. Precision Trade-off: High sensitivity did not guarantee high precision.
Some sessions, such as P08 UI1, were highly sensitive (capturing every video event) but had
very low precision, meaning that most of the GSR peaks detected were ’false alarms’ with
no visible correlate.
Table 5 presents a selection of these individual scores to illustrate the broad spectrum of alignment
observed in the study, from the highest overall correspondence (P10 UI1) to examples of the
sensitivity/precision trade-off (P08 UI1) and sessions with generally low alignment (P02 UI1).

7.5
The Influence of the Test Environment on Users’ Experience
This section presents the results related to Hypothesis 4, examining how the two different test
environments (Environment A: Standard Lab and Environment B: Altered) influenced the data
collected from observer ratings (SAM), self-reported questionnaires, and physiological and be-
havioural patterns. The section begins with a descriptive overview of the data, followed by a
formal statistical analysis to test for significant differences.

To formally test Hypothesis 4, a series of non-parametric Mann-Whitney U tests, as explained in
Section 6.3.6 were conducted to compare key metrics between Environment A and Environment B.
The results of these tests, including the median scores for each group and the calculated p-values,
are summarised in Table 13.

The analysis identified statistically significant differences between the two environments for all
subjective and affective measures. The distribution of scores for Needle/Pen Confidence was sig-
nificantly different (p = 0.047), with the median score being higher in Environment B (Mdn = 2.0)
than in Environment A (Mdn = 1.0). Significant differences were also found in the distributions
of observer-rated Overall Arousal scores (p = 0.039) and Overall Valence scores (p = 0.018).
In contrast, the tests found no statistically significant difference in the frequency of recorded events.
The per-participant counts of Total GSR Peaks (p = 1.000) and Total Video Reactions (p = 0.299)
were not significantly different between Environment A and Environment B.
These results provide statistical support for Hypothesis 4, indicating that the test environment had
a major effect on subjective and affective measures, but not on the overall frequency of recorded
physiological or behavioural reactions.
68
==PAGE:83==
Results and Analysis
Key Takeaways for Results and Analysis
• A primary finding is the quantitative discrepancy between physiological arousal (GSR peaks)
and visibly observed user reactions; over 60% of detected GSR peaks had no corresponding
observable video reaction.
• Individual participant data showed considerable variation in the alignment between GSR and
video, with some exhibiting high arousal without expressive behaviour, and others showing
clear correlations between peaks and subtle observable behaviours. Formal assessment also
confirmed wide ranges in precision and sensitivity scores, indicating a general lack of strong,
consistent alignment.
• Observer-coded emotional states (SAM ratings) and self-reported experiences often indicated
emotional neutrality, yet were frequently accompanied by GSR peaks, suggesting unexpressed
internal arousal.
• The test environment influenced both observer-rated emotional states and physiological
arousal patterns. Environment A showed a substantially higher number of false positives
(GSR peaks without video reaction) compared to Environment B.
69
==PAGE:84==
Discussion
8
Discussion
Building on the results presented in the previous section, this section provides a discussion of
the findings. The section is structured to guide from a detailed interpretation of the study’s core
findings to a broader reflection on its methodological implications and future potential. To provide
a clear overview of this argumentative flow, a visual structure is presented in Figure 25.
Figure 25: An overview of the Discussion section’s structure, showing the progression from inter-
preting the findings to discussing their broader implications.
As outlined in the figure, the discussion begins with a triangulated assessment and a detailed
interpretation of the study’s outcomes for its four hypotheses. Following this analysis, the focus
broadens to contextualise the study within the broader field of human factors (HF) research.
The section then offers a critical reflection on the study’s methodology, including its limitations,
potential biases, and key ethical considerations. Finally, the discussion concludes by proposing
concrete recommendations for future work and offering final thoughts on the study’s implications.
8.1
Triangulated Assessment of the Hypotheses
A triangulation of the physiological, observational, and self-reported data allows for a direct assess-
ment of the study’s four hypotheses. This section provides a summary of these outcomes, which
will be explored in greater detail in the subsequent sections.
H1: Supported by Descriptive Evidence. The findings indicated that GSR is a sensitive
tool for detecting task-related changes in physiological arousal. The descriptive data consistently
showed that arousal peaks occurred during key usability tasks, such as needle attachment and
70
==PAGE:85==
Discussion
priming (see Table 4), suggesting that GSR can successfully identify moments of cognitive or
physical engagement.
H2: Supported by Descriptive Evidence. The results clearly showed that video observation
does not capture all physiological arousal. The descriptive statistics revealed that an extensive
majority of GSR peaks (over 60% for both user interfaces, see Table 6) had no matching behavioural
cue. This indicates that a substantial portion of user arousal is not externally visible.
H3:
Unsupported by Statistical Evidence.
A reliable relationship between self-reported
experience and GSR arousal was not found. A formal statistical analysis using a binomial test
was conducted on the data from Environment A (p=0.232), Environment B (p=0.386), and the
combined dataset (p=0.104). In all three analyses, the results were not statistically significant.
H4: Supported by Statistical Evidence (Nuanced Finding). The hypothesis that the test
environment influences the cognitive experience received partial and specific statistical support.
A series of Mann-Whitney U tests revealed a statistically significant effect of the environment
on subjective and affective measures (including confidence, valence, and arousal), but not on the
overall frequency of GSR peaks or video reactions.
Triangulating these individual outcomes provides a holistic view of the usability as a multi-layered
phenomenon.
The findings from H1 and H2 establish GSR as a sensitive measure of internal
arousal that is often invisible to an observer. However, the statistically unsupported H3 demon-
strates that this internal physiological state does not reliably correlate with a user’s conscious,
self-reported experience. Finally, the nuanced results of H4 suggest that while the testing envi-
ronment can alter a user’s subjective and emotional state, it may not change the raw frequency
of these underlying physiological or behavioural events. Taken together, this suggests that physio-
logical arousal, observable behaviour, and subjective perception are three distinct, complementary
aspects of usability that cannot be fully understood in isolation.
The following sections will now discuss these findings in greater depth, following the order of the
hypotheses.
71
==PAGE:86==
Discussion
8.2
Detecting Arousal with GSR
Hypothesis 1 proposed that GSR measurements can detect task-related changes in physiological
arousal during interactions with the pen injectors.
The findings from this study support this,
positioning GSR as a valuable tool for uncovering moments of user engagement that are not
captured by traditional usability metrics alone.
This aligns with a known deficiency in some conventional evaluation methods, which can neglect
the user’s internal cognitive and emotional state [45]. The integration of GSR provides a method
to identify these episodes of heightened arousal or cognitive load, offering richer and more objective
insights into usability [9][5].
The results of this study demonstrate the value of GSR in two key ways. First, the descriptive
analysis in Section 7.2 showed that physiological peaks were consistently recorded during the most
critical phases of device interaction, such as needle handling and dose priming (see Table 4). This
indicates that GSR is a sensitive tool for flagging interactions that require increased physical or
cognitive effort.
Second, and more importantly, the analysis revealed that GSR uncovers a substantial number of
arousal events that are otherwise invisible. As detailed in Table 6, a total of 135 GSR peaks were
detected across both user interfaces. Of these, a compelling majority, 53 peaks for UI1 (71.6%)
and 39 for UI2 (63.9%), had no corresponding observable reaction on video. This finding directly
shows that a large portion of physiological user response is missed by observation alone.
A key methodological consideration in leveraging this sensitivity is selecting an appropriate peak
detection threshold, as mentioned in Section 6.3.3. To ensure the reliability of the findings, an
exploratory analysis was conducted comparing different thresholds. A high threshold of 0.1 µS
proved to be too insensitive, failing to register peaks even during tasks where participants showed
clear observable reactions on video. Conversely, a low threshold of 0.01 µS was overly sensitive,
generating a large number of peaks, many of which occurred during periods of inaction and were
likely attributable to signal noise. The threshold of 0.05 µS was ultimately selected as it provided
the optimal balance, reliably identifying peaks during expected moments of engagement while
filtering out most of the apparent noise. This process highlights the importance of selecting pa-
72
==PAGE:87==
Discussion
rameters carefully in GSR analysis to ensure that the detected physiological events are both valid
and meaningful.
Furthermore, the capacity of GSR to detect this task-related arousal was evident even in sessions
that otherwise appeared smooth.
For instance, as detailed in the analysis of discrepancies in
Section 7.3 and illustrated in Figure 24, a large proportion of these false positives occurred during
task-relevant physical actions. This suggests that even when a user does not make an observable
error, the device interaction can still elicit a measurable physiological response. This ability to
reveal ’silent’ moments of user effort is a key contribution of integrating GSR into usability testing.
8.3
Limitations of Video Observation in Capturing Arousal
Hypothesis 2 proposed that video observations through ordinary usability data analysis will not
cover all physiological arousal measured via GSR.
The results provide clear quantitative support for Hypothesis 2. The summary data in Table 6
and 7 in Section 7.3 shows that for both UIs, a suggestive majority of physiological arousal events
(71.6% for UI1 and 63.9% for UI2) were not accompanied by any observable reaction. This finding
confirms the primary assertion of H2: traditional observation has a blind spot and fails to capture
most of the user’s internal physiological experience.
However, the data also reveals the inverse relationship, which is equally important for interpreting
the results. Over half of all video-observed reactions (54.3% for UI1 and 55.8% for UI2) were
not detected by the GSR, resulting in a large number of false negatives. This demonstrates that
while observation is incomplete, GSR is also not a comprehensive replacement. The overall overlap
between the methods (45%) proves that they are complementary, not redundant, each capturing
different aspects of the user’s experience.
The consistency of this pattern across both devices
further suggests this is a fundamental methodological finding, not an artefact of a specific design.
8.3.1
Insights from Non-Observable GSR Peaks
The finding that a majority of GSR peaks occurred without any overt behavioural indicators, a
central result of this study as detailed with in Section 7.3.2 and quantified in Figure 13 and Table
6, suggests the detection of subtle cognitive feedback. This empirically confirms that observational
73
==PAGE:88==
Discussion
methods can overlook what Longo (2018) describes as the ’cognitive and emotional state of the
users’ [45]. Similarly, the high frequency of peaks observed during needle attachment tasks (1.3
and 1.5), as shown in Table 4 in Section 7.2, likely reflects the increased cognitive and physical load
associated with fine motor coordination under perceived evaluation. This reveals subtle moments
of user engagement or uncertainty, which are indicative of fluctuations in cognitive load, a direct
link that researchers, such as Setz et al. (2010), have demonstrated is possible to measure with
wearable electroencephalography (EEG) devices [11].
As established in Section 2.1, capturing these otherwise undetected instances of cognitive load
provides a more comprehensive view of the user’s experience. Furthermore, as noted in Section 2.4,
this method is highly relevant to formative testing because it can help flag potential design flaws.
This aligns perfectly with the foundational applications of electrodermal activity measurement in
applied psychology and human factors (HF) research, as detailed by Boucsein (2012) [9].
Identifying these subtle moments of hesitation and cognitive engagement, even when they do not
result in a use error, can inform product design and improve the clarity of, for example, the
instructions for use (IFU). These reactions underscore that physiological data should be seen as
valuable indicators of cognitive or emotional engagement that are otherwise invisible. Crucially,
they should not be interpreted solely as markers of stress or failure. This aligns with the inherent
limitations of the technology, specifically the arousal-valence-ambiguity discussed in Section 2.4.
This limitation, well-documented in foundational research on the topic [32], clarifies that GSR
measures general physiological arousal and cannot, on its own, distinguish between positive states,
such as excitement, and negative ones, like anxiety.
8.3.2
GSR Signal Processing and Participant Patterns
While the previous sections established the quantitative discrepancy between GSR and video data,
a qualitative analysis of individual participants provides deeper insight into why this discrepancy
occurs. The four participants showcased in the Results section (Figures 17-20) were not just vari-
ables; they represent distinct patterns of user response that highlight why a multi-modal approach
is so crucial.
Participants P02 and P04 represent a more moderate or ’subtle’ response pattern. Their overall
physiological reactivity was lower, yet their GSR traces still clearly show distinct peaks occurring
74
==PAGE:89==
Discussion
at key moments of interaction. These peaks were often not accompanied by strong emotional
expressions, but rather by looks of concentration or minor hesitations. This demonstrates that
even for less physiologically reactive individuals, GSR remains sensitive enough to flag specific
points of interest that might otherwise be overlooked as simple pauses in the user’s workflow.
P05 exhibits significant physiological arousal with minimal outward expression. Their data showed
a high tonic Skin Conductance Level (SCL), meaning their baseline level of arousal was consis-
tently elevated, suggesting a state of sustained engagement or alertness throughout the test. In
addition, they exhibited frequent, prominent phasic peaks (SCRs), which are sharp, momentary
spikes indicating strong physiological reactions to specific events. The critical finding for P05 is
that this ’loud’ physiological signal was paired with a consistently neutral or focused facial expres-
sion on video. This suggests the participant is experiencing cognitive load internally but is either
not consciously aware of it or is actively regulating their external expression. Without the GSR
data, this participant’s experience would have been misinterpreted as smooth and effortless.
P09 represents responses where physiological and observational data are well-aligned. In their case,
distinct GSR peaks corresponded with subtle but observable tells, such as a furrowed brow or a
momentary pause during a task. This pattern is valuable because it serves as a clear example that
GSR peaks can directly mirror observable moments of concentration or slight difficulty.
These examples collectively illustrate that different users manifest stress and cognitive load in
profoundly different ways. Relying solely on video observation may lead to an accurate assessment
for an ’expressive’ user like P09, but may result in an inaccurate one for a participant like P05.
This highlights the primary strength of using GSR: it allows for a more equitable and accurate
assessment of the usability across different personality types and styles of emotional expression.
8.3.3
Implications for Usability Testing
A central implication of this study’s findings is the inherent limitation of relying solely on video
observation to assess the usability. The data consistently reveals a ’blind spot’, where a user’s
outward appearance does not match their internal physiological state.
The most compelling evidence for this is the analysis comparing observer ratings of emotion with
GSR peaks, as detailed in the Results (Table 8) in Section 7.3.4. During critical tasks, such as
75
==PAGE:90==
Discussion
priming the needle (Task 2.1) and performing the injection (Task 2.3), participants were frequently
observed by others as looking calm or focused. However, the physiological data for these same
tasks showed a high number of arousal peaks, indicating an internal effort that was not outwardly
visible.
This phenomenon, where a user appears composed while their body shows signs of arousal, was also
evident at the individual level. The qualitative analysis of participants, such as P05 (Figure 19),
revealed a user with a ’loud’ physiological signal (high arousal) but a consistently neutral external
expression. This suggests that an observer, relying solely on video, might conclude that a task is
proceeding smoothly, while the user is actually experiencing a high degree of hidden cognitive load
or concentration.
These findings have two clear implications for usability testing. First, they confirm that video-
based analysis alone cannot provide a complete account of a user’s internal state. Second, they
position GSR as a valuable complementary tool capable of identifying these latent, non-conscious
reactions that are missed by observation.
The solution, therefore, is not to replace observational analysis, which remains crucial for iden-
tifying use errors and understanding context. Instead, the findings advocate for augmenting this
traditional methodology. By integrating objective, physiological measures like GSR, researchers
can create a more robust, multimodal evaluation that captures both the observable behaviour and
the underlying physiological effort, leading to a more complete and accurate understanding of the
usability.
8.3.4
The Limitations of GSR and the Need for Context
While the previous section established GSR as a solution for the limitations of observation, it is
equally important to recognise that GSR has its own constraints. The data reveal that it cannot
function as a standalone measure for two critical reasons.
First, the results show that GSR failed to detect a considerable portion of usability events that
were identified through video observation. Across all sessions, there were 89 instances of such false
negatives, where a behavioural cue was observed on video without any corresponding GSR peak
(as shown in Figure 15). This low sensitivity is formally quantified in Section 7.3.1, which shows
76
==PAGE:91==
Discussion
the mean sensitivity was only 0.41 for UI1 and 0.31 for UI2. This implies that GSR may miss
some of the most consequential usability events.
Second, the inverse is also true: GSR peaks that lack contextual video footage offer limited ex-
planatory value. The analysis identified 135 false positives in total (Figure 15). The breakdown of
these events, shown in Figure 16, is crucial for interpretation.
The fact that a majority of these false positives (88 out of 135) occurred during task-relevant
physical actions suggests that these are not random noise. Instead, they likely reflect the physical
effort or cognitive load required to perform the task (e.g., applying force, fine motor control), even
when the user shows no outward sign of struggle.
The remaining 47 unexplained peaks represent the actual ambiguity and challenge of the method.
These could be tied to purely internal cognitive load (e.g., confusion while reading the IFU) or they
could be measurement artefacts. Without more data, their cause is uncertain. This highlights a
critical duality: GSR can identify potentially hidden user difficulties, but it also generates data that
requires careful contextual analysis to be meaningful. This reaffirms that GSR must be integrated
with observation to be interpreted reliably.
Therefore, the analysis reaffirms the importance of combining GSR with traditional video-based
usability methods. While GSR can enhance the detection of subtle user reactions that may go
unnoticed in video analysis, it must be integrated with observational data to ensure comprehensive
and meaningful interpretation of user behaviour.
8.3.5
Individual Differences in Observability of Emotional Reactions
It is also crucial to consider how potential sources of bias may influence participant behaviour.
For instance, some participants may mask or regulate their emotional responses due to the artifi-
cial nature of the study setting, or perceived performance pressure, a phenomenon known as the
Hawthorne effect [35]. This is one of several potential biases considered in more detail in Section
8.7.3.
The results of this study clearly illustrate that individuals vary significantly in their emotional
expressiveness. This qualitative variability is shown clearly in the comparative analysis in Figures
17 through 20. The contrast between P05, who showed high physiological arousal while appearing
77
==PAGE:92==
Discussion
calm externally, and P09, whose arousal and subtle behavioural cues were well-aligned, provides a
powerful, human-level explanation for the quantitative discrepancies found in the data. It directly
challenges the validity of using observation alone, as this would lead to a misinterpretation of the
usability for less expressive individuals.
This individual variability is not merely qualitative; it is quantitatively evident across the entire
cohort.
For instance, the data for P10 (UI1) showed high alignment between physiology and
behaviour (precision 0.86, sensitivity 1.00). In contrast, P02 (UI1) showed a near-total disconnect
(precision 0.09, sensitivity 0.14), a contrast detailed in Table 5 (Section 7.3.1). This highlights
the quantitative spread visible in the participant-by-participant breakdown in Figure 13 (Section
7.3.2), which shows how the number of ’hidden’ GSR peaks differs significantly from person to
person. This variability is further confirmed by the wide distribution of precision and sensitivity
scores across all users, as shown in Figure 12 (Section 7.3.1).
Ultimately, GSR helps account for these individual differences by capturing hidden physiological
responses. This reinforces its role as a vital complement to behavioural methods, allowing for a
more equitable analysis that does not penalise or misinterpret participants who are naturally less
expressive.
8.3.6
Emotional State Assessment using the SAM Framework
The choice to use The Self-Assessment Manikin (SAM) was motivated by the limitations of GSR
measurements discussed in Section 2.4. While GSR is effective at detecting changes in physiological
arousal, it cannot differentiate between types of arousal, such as positive excitement versus negative
stress.
The SAM framework addresses this limitation by capturing both arousal and valence
(emotional positivity and negativity), allowing for a more nuanced interpretation of the emotional
experience.
The results from the SAM framework support this approach and reveal significant differences
between the test environments, as presented in the boxplots in Figure 21. These results show that
while the median emotional experience was often similar across environments (typically a neutral
score of 5 for both valence and arousal), participants in Environment A displayed more variation
in their feelings. This is evident in the wider interquartile ranges for their SAM scores, particularly
for valence.
78
==PAGE:93==
Discussion
This means that while the ’average’ experience was neutral, individual participants in Environment
A had more polarised emotional reactions, with some feeling much more negative and others more
positive. In contrast, the scores in Environment B were more tightly clustered, suggesting a more
consistent and stable emotional experience for all participants. The line graphs showing individual
emotional journeys (Figure 26 and 27) also visually illustrate this greater emotional variation in
Environment A.
SAM scores provide additional support for H2 by demonstrating emotional variation that is not
directly visible through GSR measurement alone. As H2 proposed, conventional usability methods
may fail to capture all underlying arousal, and the SAM results support this by revealing broader
affective trends that extend beyond what is behaviourally observable.
Figure 21 from the results analysis illustrates this discrepancy clearly. While the median valence
and arousal scores were consistently neutral (a score of 5) across most interfaces and environments,
the emotional spread was notably wider in Environment A. For example, with UI2, the valence
scores in Environment A had a wider interquartile range (3 to 6) compared to the tightly clustered
scores in Environment B (where the interquartile range was zero, with the median, lower, and
upper quartiles all at 5).
This indicates that participants in Environment A had more polarised emotional experiences,
some much more negative, some more positive, suggesting moments of internal discomfort or
engagement that were not always accompanied by uniform behavioural cues. By contrast, the
more emotionally balanced scores from Environment B support the observation of more relaxed
and consistent task behaviour. This highlights how subtle emotional responses, especially those
occurring in Environment A, might have gone unnoticed without the structured SAM framework.
Reflecting on the use of the SAM framework, it is important to acknowledge its inherent subjec-
tivity. The pilot testing phase highlighted the general challenge of obtaining reliable physiological
data, prompting an investigation into signal discrepancies caused by factors such as loose elec-
trodes and potential movement artefacts (Pilot Test 3). This reinforced the need for a precise and
cautious protocol for coding observable behaviour. Accordingly, the method established during the
formative test design did not automatically classify all movement or concentration as ’suspicious’.
Instead, in this study, we acted as observers who assigned SAM scores based on clear behavioural
79
==PAGE:94==
Discussion
cues, such as facial expressions, body posture, and verbal utterances. To further mitigate this
subjectivity, inter-rater agreement was evaluated during the scoring phase. This evaluation con-
firmed a consistent alignment between the two researchers’ ratings for both valence and arousal,
enhancing the reliability of the SAM framework’s application despite its subjective nature.
Overall, the SAM boxplots validate H2 by underscoring that subjective emotional states, par-
ticularly those involving arousal, can diverge from visible behaviour. These internal experiences,
reflected in the broader score distribution, demonstrate the importance of incorporating both phys-
iological and affective assessment tools to understand usability during medical device interaction
fully.
8.3.7
Linking Emotional State and Physiological Arousal
The relationship between the observed emotional state (SAM scores) and physiological arousal
(GSR peaks) proved to be complex, as detailed in the comparative data in Table 8. The findings
suggest that while periods of neutral or content behaviour often corresponded with low physio-
logical arousal, there were notable exceptions where this was not the case. For instance, in Task
1.1 (Read IFU), only 3 GSR peaks were recorded alongside nine neutral-coded cases of behaviour,
showing good alignment. In sharp contrast, Task 2.3 (Inject into pad) was accompanied by 12
GSR peaks despite having a high number of neutral-coded behaviours (13), indicating a significant
physiological response without corresponding visible emotional expression.
This discrepancy raises important questions and suggests that factors beyond observable emotion,
such as the unobserved cognitive load of performing the injection correctly or the physical effort
involved, likely contributed to the heightened GSR responses. It means that even when a user
appears calm or focused, they may be experiencing a high degree of internal processing or strain.
To better understand the underlying causes of these mismatches, further examination could take
several forms:
• Granular Qualitative Analysis: A micro-analysis of the video footage from high-discrepancy
tasks (like Task 2.1 and 2.3) could reveal subtle, un-coded behaviours or hesitations that
align with the GSR peaks.
• Addition of other Biometrics: Future studies could integrate heart rate variability (HRV),
80
==PAGE:95==
Discussion
which can help differentiate between arousal caused by negative stress versus positive excite-
ment or deep concentration.
• Cued-Recall Debriefing: A powerful future method would be to use cued-recall, where par-
ticipants are shown their video and GSR data post-test and asked to reflect on what they
were thinking or feeling at the exact moment of a specific peak.
For now, this inconsistency suggests that self-reported questionnaire answers and behavioural cues
do not fully capture a user’s internal state. It underscores the value of GSR in uncovering this
latent arousal, even as it highlights the interpretive challenges that arise when physiological and
behavioural data diverge.
8.3.8
Assessment of GSR Classification Performance
The analysis comparing SAM-coded emotional states to GSR peaks revealed a nuanced and oc-
casionally inconsistent relationship, as detailed in the task-by-task breakdown in Table 8. While
neutral or calm emotional states often correlated with low physiological activity, the notable ex-
ceptions, where GSR peaks occurred despite an absence of observable emotion, support the well-
established challenge that subjective reports and facial expressions may not fully capture a user’s
internal state [45][41].
These discrepancies highlight the complexity of interpreting emotional
engagement during usability testing and underscore the need for multiple data sources.
To better quantify the practical alignment between GSR data and behavioural observations, the
precision and sensitivity metrics were calculated for each session, as reported in Section 7.3.1.
The results showed considerable individual variation, with Figure 12 illustrating a wide spread of
scores. Precision ranged from 0.0 to 0.86 and sensitivity from 0.0 to 1.0, with six sessions recording
no alignment at all.
To fully interpret these results, it is important to understand what the precision and sensitivity
scores signify in this context:
• A Precision Score indicates how ’trustworthy’ a GSR peak is as a sign of an observable
problem.
– A score of 1.0 would mean every single GSR peak corresponded to a video-observed
reaction (zero false alarms).
81
==PAGE:96==
Discussion
– A score of 0.0 means no GSR peaks corresponded to a video-observed reaction.
– Our low mean precision (0.2) means that roughly 4 out of 5 GSR peaks were not matched
by a visible user reaction.
• A Sensitivity Score indicates how ’comprehensive’ the GSR is at detecting the problems
we could see on video.
– A score of 1.0 (as seen in a few sessions like P10 UI1) means the GSR sensor successfully
detected every single event the observers saw on video.
– A score of 0.0 (as seen in six sessions) means the GSR sensor detected none of the
events seen on video.
– Our low mean sensitivity (0.3-0.4) means the GSR sensor failed to register a physiolog-
ical response for more than half of all the events observed on video.
Crucially, it is essential to consider what the ’best’ result would be for this study. A perfect score,
where both precision and sensitivity were 1.0, would have refuted the study’s core premise. It
would have meant that GSR and video observation are redundant because they capture the same
information. Similarly, scores of zero across the board would have suggested the GSR data was
meaningless noise, uncorrelated with the usability.
Therefore, the specific findings from this assessment, low mean precision and sensitivity with a wide
distribution of scores across participants, provide the strongest possible support for H2 precisely
because they show a complex and imperfect alignment. The results are in a meaningful middle
ground:
• The low precision is caused by a high number of false positives, which is the direct evidence
that GSR captures internal arousal missed by observation. This directly confirms the core
of H2.
• The low sensitivity is caused by a high number of false negatives, which proves that GSR
cannot replace observation, as it fails to detect many visible usability issues. This reinforces
the conclusion that the two methods are not interchangeable but are complementary.
82
==PAGE:97==
Discussion
8.4
Self-Reported Experience Versus GSR
Hypothesis 3 proposed that a participant’s subjective report of task difficulty or low confidence would
correlate with increased physiological arousal as measured by GSR.
To test this thoroughly, the data from Environment A and Environment B were analysed separately
first. This was done to respect the overall study design, which investigates the influence of context
(Hypothesis 4), and to determine if the relationship between the two measures was consistent across
different conditions. Second, an analysis was performed on all the data from both environments.
The rationale for combining the datasets was to increase statistical power, providing a better
opportunity to detect a subtle, overall trend if one exists.
As detailed in the Results section (Section 7.4), this comprehensive analysis yielded a clear out-
come. None of the tests revealed a statistically significant relationship: not for Environment A
(p = 0.232), not for Environment B (p = 0.386), and not even for the pooled dataset with its
larger sample size (p = 0.104). The hypothesis was therefore robustly unsupported by the data.
This consistent lack of a statistically significant correlation is, in itself, a critical finding. It suggests
that self-reported experience and physiological arousal may not be interchangeable measures of
usability; they appear to represent two distinct phenomena within the context of this study. This
apparent decoupling offers two key interpretations:
First, it could indicate that GSR does not function as a simple ’difficulty-meter’.
Instead of
directly mirroring a user’s conscious assessment of a task, the physiological arousal it measures
could be triggered by a broader range of factors. These might include underlying cognitive load,
the physical exertion of handling the device, or even background stress, none of which may be
explicitly articulated by the user in a post-task questionnaire.
Second, this result also seems to point towards the known limitations of self-report data.
A
user’s retrospective rating is a conscious, remembered perception, which may not fully capture the
nuances of their more subtle, in-the-moment physiological state.
Therefore, the non-significant result for H3 should not be seen as invalidating either measurement
method. Instead, it appears to clarify their complementary roles. Self-report seems to capture a
83
==PAGE:98==
Discussion
user’s conscious perception, while GSR provides insight into unconscious, physiological reactions.
The very fact that these two data streams do not consistently align in this study highlights the
value of a multimodal approach, suggesting that to gain a richer understanding of the usability, it
is beneficial to consider both the articulated story from the user and the physiological story from
their body.
8.5
The Influence of the Test Environment
Hypothesis 4 predicted that the test environments would change the level of arousal and the be-
havioural responses of users.
The findings provide strong, but nuanced, statistical support for this hypothesis, confirming that
the test environment has a profound impact on the usability. This aligns with existing literature
from authors such as Batliner [51] and Sauer [38], who argue that sterile laboratory conditions can
fail to capture the reality of device use. As the analysis in Section 7.5.4 revealed, the environment’s
influence was most pronounced on the quality of the users’ experience, their subjective feelings
and emotional states, rather than on the raw quantity of physiological or behavioural events.
8.5.1
The Mitigating Effect of Environment on Subjective and Affective Experience
The most unmistakable evidence of the environment’s impact comes from measures of user feel-
ings and emotions. As shown in the statistical summary (Table 13), participants in the altered
Environment B reported statistically significantly higher confidence with the needles and pens
(p = 0.047). This confirms the trend observed in the descriptive charts (Figure 23), where scores
in Environment B were more consistently positive.
The observer-rated emotional data support this subjective finding. The analysis revealed a sta-
tistically significant difference in the overall distributions of both Valence scores (p = 0.018) and
Arousal scores (p = 0.039). This gives statistical weight to the patterns seen in the boxplots (Fig-
ure 21). While the median emotion was neutral in both settings, the experience in Environment B
was more positive and emotionally stable. This suggests that the altered environment acted as a
psychological buffer, mitigating negative feelings and fostering a more consistent emotional state
during the tasks.
84
==PAGE:99==
Discussion
8.5.2
The Influence on Event Frequency and Behavioural Patterns
Interestingly, the effect on how users felt did not translate to a difference in how many times
they reacted. The Mann-Whitney U tests found no statistically significant difference between the
environments in either the number of Total GSR Peaks per participant (p = 1.000) or the number
of Total Video Reactions (p = 0.299) (Table 13).
This distinction is a key finding of the study. It suggests that the environment’s influence was
qualitative rather than quantitative. The ’home-like’ setting did not necessarily reduce the number
of moments that caused a physiological or behavioural reaction. Still, it appears to have funda-
mentally changed the emotional context and perception of those moments. This interpretation is
further supported by the analysis of the unobserved GSR peaks (Figure 24), which showed that the
standard lab (Environment A) prompted more ’physical’ hidden reactions, while Environment B
was associated with more ’mental’ ones, suggesting a different cognitive processing of the tasks.
So, the support for Hypothesis 4 is nuanced. The test environment did not alter the frequency of
user reactions. Still, it fundamentally shaped the stability of their emotions and their conscious
perception of usability, reinforcing the critical role of context in medical device testing.
8.6
Positioning This Study within the Broader Field
This study contributes to a growing body of research exploring the intersection of feedback and
physiological monitoring, as seen in works such as Duffy and Lightner [23], Töth et al. [43], and Seo
et al. [69]. While traditional formative usability methods, such as task observation, think-aloud
protocols, and post-task questionnaires, remain indispensable according to regulatory compliances
like IEC 62366-1 [1], there is an increasing recognition that such approaches may not fully capture
the cognitive and emotional burden experienced by users during interaction with medical devices
[23][43][69]. By integrating GSR into an empirical usability study, this work takes a pragmatic
step toward operationalising emotion-informed usability evaluation.
Unlike prior studies that focused solely on simulated stress scenarios or entertainment-based stim-
uli, such as Tóth et al., who used Paced Auditory Serial Addition Test (PASAT) to induce cognitive
stress [43] (for other examples see the authors Seo et al. [69] and Duffy and Lighter [23]), this
investigation embedded GSR into a contextually rich, domain-relevant task: simulating pen injec-
85
==PAGE:100==
Discussion
tor use in controlled but ecologically valid environments. This methodological shift provides new
insights into how moment-to-moment emotional states can be mapped onto procedural steps in the
use of medical devices. Moreover, the study demonstrates that the dual use of both explicit and
implicit data, via observed use errors, GSR peaks, and SAM-coded affect, represents a significant
advancement in multimodal usability assessment.
The study’s findings reinforce the call for a more nuanced definition of ’ease of use’ in human factors
(HF) research, one that goes beyond errors and completion times to also include internalised stress,
uncertainty, or cognitive load. In doing so, this study complements existing regulatory guidance
(e.g., IEC 62366-1 [1]) by offering a more holistic view of how users interact with medical devices,
especially in contexts where safety and emotional comfort are intertwined. Future research building
on these foundations could contribute to a more robust, standardised methodology for integrating
physiological data into medical device validation and approval processes.
8.7
Methodological Considerations, Limitations, and Future Directions
The preceding sections confirmed that galvanic skin response (GSR) can reveal subtle, often hid-
den, physiological responses during usability tasks, responses that are not always visible through
observation or captured in self-report questionnaires. This supports the growing argument that
multimodal approaches are essential for evaluating complex interactions with medical devices.
However, demonstrating that GSR can provide new insights is only part of the story. It is equally
important to critically assess the methodological trade-offs, practical implementation challenges,
and ethical implications of introducing physiological monitoring into usability workflows.
This next part of the discussion reflects on the lessons learned during the design and execution
of the study. It includes expert validation from industry professionals, identifies key limitations,
explores potential sources of bias, and discusses the broader ethical and regulatory context in
which GSR might be applied. These reflections are crucial, not only for interpreting the results
responsibly, but also for shaping how future studies might adopt, refine, or standardise the use of
physiological metrics in usability testing.
86
==PAGE:101==
Discussion
8.7.1
Validation Insights from Industry Experts
To further validate the study’s methodology, semi-structured interviews were conducted with a
senior human factors (HF) engineer and a senior quality assurance (QA) consultant. The HF
engineer recognised GSR as a promising approach to bridge the gap between subjective qualitative
feedback and objective quantitative data. He highlighted that GSR can reveal moments of emo-
tional arousal or increased cognitive load that are not apparent to observers, although he cautioned
that its practical implementation requires HF engineers to be trained in basic signal processing
and data management. It was estimated that including GSR could add 15-20 hours of effort to a
typical formative usability study.
From the QA perspective, a consistent theme was the need for robust evidence demonstrating that
the GSR method delivers relevant, reproducible, and actionable data. For acceptance in regulated
workflows, GSR must be shown to detect arousal in a way that is meaningful and aligned with user
performance. This would require documented controls, standardised tables of expected values,
and formalised procedures included within a company’s Quality Management System (QMS).
Specifically, the QA expert identified four key steps for implementation: 1) Validation of the
method’s effectiveness, 2) Evidence generation of expected results, 3) Template and procedure
development for internal documentation, and 4) Pilot execution with a full audit trail. The need
for a complete audit trail, including an approved test plan, protocol, post-study report, and securely
stored anonymised raw data, was stressed as essential for regulatory acceptance.
8.7.2
Challenges and Limitations
While this study has demonstrated benefits, it is essential to recognise its limitations. The physio-
logical data collected via GSR are inherently subject to individual variability (e.g., skin hydration,
age, and medication).
While efforts were made to standardise the environment and establish
individual baselines, the potential for such variability to affect measurements remains a constraint.
The technical characteristics of the Shimmer3 GSR+ warrant careful consideration. Its high sam-
pling rate is advantageous for capturing both tonic (slow-changing baseline) and phasic (rapid,
event-related) components of the signal. However, this sensitivity means the device is prone to cap-
turing artefacts from movement or pressure changes, which can masquerade as genuine responses.
Distinguishing these artefacts required careful signal processing, which can itself introduce sub-
87
==PAGE:102==
Discussion
jectivity. One significant limitation encountered was baseline drift due to sweat accumulation,
which can compromise the accuracy of the measurement. Our protocol included an initial baseline
measurement to normalise the data, but this remains a challenge. Additionally, one participant’s
GSR recording failed to produce any discernible peaks, highlighting that physiological differences
can occasionally lead to atypical outputs.
A further limitation concerns the selection of literature search platforms. Our institutional affil-
iation influenced our decision to choose DTU Findit. Google Scholar was included for its broad
coverage, despite potential indexing inconsistencies, and ScienceDirect was selected for its peer-
reviewed focus, primarily covering the Elsevier portfolio. This pragmatic combination may have
inadvertently excluded relevant research.
Finally, the representativeness of the participant sample is a notable limitation. The use of par-
ticipants with prior knowledge of medical devices means the group may possess higher baseline
knowledge, confidence, or dexterity than typical end-users [2] [7]. Consequently, specific usability
challenges and stress responses that could arise in a more representative, novice sample (the IEC
62366-2 states that one need to strive for the most optimal user group [2]) may not have been
fully captured in this study, as our participants only consisted of MedTech consultants and people
working with human resources. While our participant number satisfies formative usability guide-
lines found in the IEC 62366-2 [2], it is less ideal for drawing firm conclusions about the broader
scientific application of GSR.
8.7.3
Potential Sources of Bias and Their Implications
A thorough examination of potential biases is essential in any empirical study, particularly when
multiple data streams (behavioural, self-report, and physiological) are combined [33]. Bias has the
potential to influence data collection, analysis, and interpretation, thereby limiting the validity of
the findings [33]. This part outlines the principal forms of bias relevant to this study, describes
their likely impact, and indicates measures taken to mitigate their effects.
• Selection Bias: The use of experienced employees may underestimate the frequency and
gravity of use errors and associated arousal that could manifest among novice end users.
Furthermore, while the chosen sample size satisfies IEC 62366-2:2016 recommendations for
formative usability studies (see Section 3.1), it is less suitable for generalising physiological
88
==PAGE:103==
Discussion
patterns to the full user population [33].
• Observer (Rating) Bias: The interpretation of behaviour using the SAM framework car-
ries an element of subjectivity.
To reduce this, two researchers independently rated all
participants, and inter-rater reliability was assessed to ensure consistency [33].
• Hawthorne Effect: Participants’ awareness of being observed and recorded frequently in-
duces more deliberate behaviour, potentially reducing visible signs of frustration and lowering
physiological arousal [35].
• Confirmation Bias: There was a risk of unintentionally focusing on data that supported
our hypotheses. To reduce this, we used a fixed, automated method for detecting GSR peaks
(a threshold of 0.05 µS) before looking at any of the videos [33].
• Social-Desirability Bias: Participants might have understated confusion or overstated
confidence. Inconsistency between verbal reports and GSR peaks underscores this concern
[62].
• Instrument (Measurement) Bias: Electrodermal measurements are sensitive to ambi-
ent factors.
While skin surfaces were cleaned and a baseline was used, residual noise is
unavoidable [33].
By acknowledging and reflecting on the various types of bias present in this study, we provide
essential context for understanding the results.
While certain limitations, such as participant
background, subjective scoring, and measurement noise, inevitably shape the findings, they do not
undermine the overall value of the study. Instead, they clarify where caution is needed and where
future improvements can be made.
8.7.4
Ethical Considerations and Physiological Monitoring
Ethical considerations assume particular importance in studies involving physiological data [1].
Participant consent procedures were designed to ensure individuals were fully informed about the
nature of GSR measurement and their right to withdraw. Special care was taken to anonymise all
data files. Despite these precautions, the inherently intimate nature of physiological monitoring
raises questions regarding participant autonomy and comfort. Researchers have an obligation not
only to inform participants about these possibilities but also to interpret and report physiological
89
==PAGE:104==
Discussion
data with care and nuance. As physiological monitoring becomes more common, it is crucial to
maintain a strong ethical framework that prioritises transparency, voluntary participation, and
data security.
8.7.5
Future Work
The integration of GSR into usability testing for medical devices has shown promise in capturing
user arousal and cognitive load. While the findings of this study support the feasibility and utility
of such physiological measures, they also highlight areas for further exploration and refinement.
Future work should aim to enhance the robustness, generalisability, and practical implementation
of this approach.
• Broaden Participant Diversity: Future studies should include a wider range of partici-
pants, including patients, elderly users, and those with varying levels of health literacy.
• Multimodal Physiological Measurement: Combining GSR with additional biometric
indicators such as heart rate variability (HRV), eye tracking, or facial expression analysis
could enable a more comprehensive understanding of emotional and cognitive states.
• Standardisation of Physiological Signal Interpretation: Developing validated frame-
works to interpret physiological signals would increase consistency and comparability across
studies.
• Longitudinal and Real-World Testing: Conducting longitudinal studies or in-situ eval-
uations could help determine how physiological responses evolve with repeated device use.
• Sensor Design and Wearability Optimisation: The GSR device should be adapted to
minimise interference with finger movement, improving comfort and natural hand use.
• Assessment of Test Environment Effects: Future work should systematically evalu-
ate how different test environments affect both physiological responses and user-reported
experience.
• Automation and Real-Time Analysis: Implementing machine learning models for real-
time interpretation may support adaptive testing.
90
==PAGE:105==
Discussion
• Regulatory Alignment and Industry Uptake: Further exploration is needed on how
physiological metrics can be meaningfully integrated into regulatory frameworks such as IEC
62366-1.
• Data Handling and Platform Considerations: Code used for data analysis should be
hosted in GDPR-compliant environments to ensure secure handling of sensitive data.
While this study has established a foundational understanding of the role GSR can play in MedTech
usability testing, future work should continue to build on this foundation through interdisciplinary
collaboration, technological advancements, and user-centred refinement.
Through such efforts,
physiological data can become a robust and accepted complement to traditional usability metrics,
ultimately supporting the development of safer and more intuitive medical devices.
8.7.6
Final Reflections
At the beginning of this project, we anticipated that GSR would reveal more peaks than could
be captured through traditional usability testing, and our findings ultimately confirmed this as-
sumption. The data demonstrated that GSR peaks occurred more often than visible emotional
reactions, highlighting the potential of physiological measurements to uncover hidden arousal.
However, what we did not foresee was the complexity involved in aligning and interpreting these
data sources. It became apparent that comparing GSR peaks with video reactions was far from
straightforward.
Identifying which peaks were meaningful and distinguishing them from noise
required not only technical refinement but also a deeper understanding of emotional and cognitive
theory. This led us to explore psychological frameworks such SAM, the Circumplex Model of Affect,
and the confusion matrix, tools with which we had no prior experience, but which became central
to understanding ease of use.
Another unexpected challenge was the presence of signal noise,
particularly when distinguishing movement artefacts from genuine arousal, which introduced an
additional layer of preprocessing and critical interpretation.
We also faced the unexpected challenge of signal noise, which made it difficult to distinguish
between a genuine emotional reaction and a signal caused by simply moving a finger. This meant
we had to add extra steps to clean the data and carefully consider the implications of the results.
At the same time, we continuously improved our study method based on what we learned from
91
==PAGE:106==
Discussion
early tests and participant feedback.
For example, we made the task instructions clearer and
adjusted the timing of when we distributed questionnaires.
Overall, while most of the results aligned with our original assumptions, the path to those results
was far more complex than we initially imagined. The experience has highlighted not only the
potential but also the practical challenges of integrating GSR into usability testing workflows.
Key Takeaways for Discussion
• H1 (GSR detects arousal): Supported.
GSR successfully identified physiological arousal
during key usability tasks, confirming its sensitivity for cognitive or physical engagement.
• H2 (Video doesn’t cover all arousal): Supported. A compelling majority of GSR peaks were
not visibly observed, indicating physiological arousal often goes undetected by traditional
methods.
• H3 (Self-report vs. GSR): Not Supported. There was a modest alignment between self-
reported experiences and GSR arousal, suggesting a dissociation between conscious percep-
tion and physiological response.
• H4 (Environment influence): Supported. The test environment had a significant impact on
self-reported usability, emotional states, and physiological arousal patterns, with a standard
lab setting eliciting more extreme responses.
• Practical Implication: Integrating GSR into formative usability testing provides a more
holistic understanding of ease of use by revealing hidden usability issues.
• Methodological Limitations: The study acknowledges limitations due to its small sample size
and potential biases, which limit generalisability.
92
==PAGE:107==
Conclusion
9
Conclusion
This study finds that incorporating galvanic skin response (GSR) into the usability evaluation of
pen injectors provides measurable and complementary insights into user arousal not consistently
captured by standard video-based observations. While traditional methods identified visible signs
of user difficulty, GSR revealed subtle physiological responses that signalled cognitive or emotional
strain, even during interactions that appeared smooth on the surface.
The comparison across test environments showed that test environment factors influence arousal
levels, with more immersive settings prompting clearer physiological reactions. By combining GSR
with observational data, the study offers a more nuanced understanding of ease of use, reinforcing
the value of multimodal approaches in medical device evaluation.
These findings support the
integration of physiological metrics into usability testing frameworks to enhance the informed
design and regulatory processes.

1

Executive Summary 
This formative usability study evaluated two insulin pen injectors, focusing on ease of 
use and the influence of environment. Twelve participants completed key tasks - 
needle attachment and simulated injection - across two test settings: a standard 
usability lab (environment A) and a modified version with white noise and plants to 
simulate a home environment (environment B).  
Data was collected via observation, questionnaires, and Galvanic Skin Response (GSR) 
to assess both performance and physiological arousal. Results showed that while 
Environment B generally improved perceived usability, it also slightly increased stress 
indicators in some participants. Differences in pen design, such as feedback 
mechanisms and dose knob clarity, also affected user experience.  
Findings will guide design improvements aimed at enhancing usability, safety, and 
comfort in future pen injector development.

==PAGE:124==

General Information  
 
Protocol title:  
Device Formative Usability Test Protocol. 
Primary investigators:  
Tara Skov Rudkjøbing and Hanna Juul-Andersen. 
Organisation:  
Technical University of Denmark.  
Responsibilities:  
The primary investigators are responsible for: test oversight, develop test set-up, 
develop protocol, recruiting test participants, conducting the study, moderating all 
studies on site, data collection, analysis of data, report development, and compliance 
with all applicable requirements and standards.  
Estimates test duration:  
17th of March 2025 to the 4th of April 2025.  
Data collection period:  
24th of March 2025 to the 4th of April 2025.  
Test location:  
Technolution, Hørsholm, Denmark.  
Number of participants per study:  
6.  
Number of participants in total:  
12.
