Introduction  
This formative study is part of a research thesis exploring GSR; however, its primary 
focus is on usability. The study aims to evaluate various aspects of the injector pen’s 
user interface by gathering participant feedback. The objective is to assess ease of use, 
safety, and overall user experience to inform design decisions and support the 
selection of the most effective interface elements. In addition, the tests will be in two 
different test environments to evaluate the impact.  
Scope and Objectives  
The scope of this study apply to two main components of the user interface the dose 
selector and interchangeable needle. Their interactions with the users while using this 
device will be evaluated as well as the two test environments.  
 The main objectives are:  
- 
Assess the usability of two injector pens by gathering user feedback on their 
interaction with the device.  
- 
Analyse the ease of use of each pen for both needle to pen attachment and 
deliver injection/drug, identifying strengths and areas for improvement.  
- 
Use participant insights to rank the pens and support decision-making, 
prioritising designs that enhance safety and ease of use.  
- 
Evaluate the impact of test environment.  
User Interface Description  
The user interface will be evaluated in this study. The interface of a typical pen injector 
consists of:  
Part name 
Description  
Injection button 
Used to administer the insulin dose by pressing it down. 
Dose knob 
Allows the user to set the correct insulin dose by turning 
it. 
Dose window 
Displays the selected dose of insulin to be injected. 
Pen cap 
Protects the insulin pen when not in use and prevents 
contamination. 
Drug reservoir  
Contains the drug solution to be injected. 
Rubber seal 
Provides a sterile barrier at the end of the insulin 
reservoir and is punctured by the needle during 
injection. 
Inner needle cap 
Covers and protects the needle before use to maintain 
sterility. 
Appendix
111
==PAGE:126==
7 
 
Needle 
The thin, hollow component that delivers insulin into the 
body. It is attached to the pen before injection. 
Paper tab 
The thin, hollow component that delivers insulin into the 
body. It is attached to the pen before injection. 
Outer needle cap 
Protects the needle after it has been attached to the 
pen, helping with safe handling before and after use. 
Table 1: Pen injector parts.  
 
Figure 1: Pen injector parts.  
    
Pen Injector Concepts 
There are two pen injectors for this study. These two pen injectors are chosen as they 
are different on the following parameters.  
User Interface 1:  
- 
The amount of drug being injected is chosen by turning the end of the pen. It 
does not have a clearly formed dose knob.  
- 
The amount of drug will be visible in the dose window.  
- 
There is no audible feedback when turning the dose knob.  
- 
When the pen cap is removed the drug is visible in the drug reservoir.  
- 
There is no drug in the cartridge.  
Appendix
112
==PAGE:127==
8 
 
 
Figure 2: User Interface 1.  
User Interface 2:  
- 
The amount of drug being injected is chosen by turning the end of the pen, with a 
clearly visible dose knob.  
- 
The amount of drug will be visible in the window.  
- 
The injection button will keep on going outwards with the increased amount of 
the drug chosen.  
- 
It gives audible feedback when turning the end.  
- 
When the pen cap is removed the drug is visible in the drug reservoir.  
- 
There is drug in the cartridge.

Figure 3: User Interface 2.  
Methodology 
 
Study Structure 
The test participants first reviewed and signed the consent forms before entering the 
study. The tests were intended to follow the structure:  
1. Introduction to the study and agenda, whilst wearing the GSR to get a baseline of 
each test participant.  
2. Demographic questions.  
3. Use Scenario 1: Task 1 Put on needle.   
4. Post Task 1 Put on Needle Questionnaire 
5. Use Scenario 2: Task 2 Deliver injection.   
6. Post Task 2 Deliver Injection Questionnaire 
7. Post Test Questionnaire on the GSR 
8. Dismissal.  
 
Randomization of Pen Order:  
To avoid learning effects or device familiarity influencing the results, the order in which 
participants use the pen injectors is randomised. Each participant will test both pens 
Appendix
114
==PAGE:129==
10 
 
during the session, but the assignment of which pen is used first is determined 
randomly. This ensures that any usability differences are not due to the order of use.

Test environment 
The test will be performed at Technolution in the Usability Lab. It is chosen as 
Technolution makes their usability studies in this exact room. In addition, the room has 
both lights from lamps, natural light from the window and have a home-feel.  
The same study is run two times with two changes in the use environments. The first 
test environment will be the normal Technolution way, which is referred to as test 
environment A. It is called the ‘Usability Lab’ where there is both natural light, lights 
from lamps, and a homey feeling.

The second test environment will be in the same room, however with some 
adjustments. There will be plants in the room and white noise to the maximum level 
according to the standards (see appendix 03), which will be referred to as test 
environment B. For this study the noise level was chosen to be 55 dB using the song 
‘Loud Basement Dehumidifier’ by Tmsoft’s White noise sleep sounds from the album 
‘Household Sounds for Sleep and Relaxation Volume 1’ published in 2020. This was 
chosen to simulate a home environment feel.

Use Specification  
The use specification for NovoPen II is in appendix 04 and for NovoPen 6 in appendix 05.  
 
Participants 
This study will involve 12 participants, divided into two groups of six per study round. 
Each participant will take part in a structured usability testing session, where they will 
be asked to handle and use pen injectors to inject into a Diabetic Injection Pad 
(Advanced Version). The study aims to evaluate the usability of different pen injector 
designs under varying environmental conditions, with Galvanic Skin Response (GSR) 
data collected to assess potential stress or discomfort during the tasks.   
The number of participants is according to IEC 62366-2:2016 a satisfactory sample size 
to identify usability defects (p. 95, appendix K). At six participants the cumulative 
probability of detecting a usability problem is 0.82. Therefore, the number of six 
participants per study is found satisfactory.  
The primary investigators responsibilities are to moderate behaviour and observing 
reasonable limits in the test.  It is also their responsibilities to make sure that the test 
participant feels safe during the test and that the test participant does not get hurt in 
any way. Therefore, it is also their responsibility to stop the test if use errors occur 
which is for danger to themselves or others.  
For potential risks to human subjects see appendix 06 and use specification in 
appendix 04 and 05.  
Session Activities  
All tasks using the pen injector are included and assessed in the study. Tasks are 
assessed by observing performance, collecting GSR live data, and noting verbal 
responses from participants. The study applies to both Test Environment A and Test 
Environment B. 
• Participants are NOT given the Use Scenario table with subtasks. 
• Participants are guided by the moderator, who introduces each scenario and 
observes their actions with the co-moderator.  
Appendix
120
==PAGE:135==
16 
 
• Priming & Injection are simulated → One of the pens has no drug, so participants 
will pretend to prime and pretend to inject. 
• Instructions are provided only if needed → The study assesses if users recognise 
the necessary steps independently. 
Use Scenarios 
Use Scenario 1: Needle-to-Pen Attachment 
Sub Task and ID 
Description 
1.1 Read and interpret 
the Instructions for Use 
(IFU) 
The participant reads and attempts to follow the 
Instructions for Use (IFU) before attaching the needle. 
1.2 Remove the paper 
tab 
The participant removes the paper tab without removing 
the needle from the packaging. 
1.3 Attach the needle 
The participant screws the needle onto the pen injector 
without removing the outer needle cap. 
1.4 Remove the outer 
needle cap 
The participant removes the outer needle cap carefully 
and does not discard it, as it is needed later. 
1.5 Remove and dispose 
of the inner needle cap 
The participant removes the inner needle cap and 
disposes of it safely without risk of injury. 
Table 7: Use Scenario 1.  
Use Scenario 2: Delivering the Injection 
Sub Task and ID 
Description 
2.1 Prime the needle 
The participant attempts to follow the priming procedure 
before injection. Skipping this step is considered an error. 
2.2 Set the dose 
The participant sets the dose to the correct amount and 
confirms their selection out loud if uncertain.  
2.3 Inject into the pad 
The participant pretends to inject the full dose into the 
pad. 
2.4 Put the outer needle 
cap back on 
The participant safely recaps the needle without self-
injury. 
2.5 Remove the needle 
from the pen 
The participant unscrews the needle from the pen without 
removing the cap first. 
2.6 Dispose of the 
needle 
The participant correctly disposes of the needle in the 
provided sharps container. 
Table 8: Use Scenario 2.

Table 15: Ratings for NovoPen 6 environment B.  
 
Summary of Results with Questionnaires  
The formative test study was conducted in two different environments: Environment A, 
the default Usability Lab setting at Technolution, and Environment B, which included 
additional white noise (55 dB) and plants to simulate a more homely atmosphere. Each 
participant performed tasks, and their responses were captured using a scoring system 
ranging from -2 to +2, where the y-axis represents the number of votes for each score.  
Put Needle on Pen 
Appendix
126
==PAGE:141==
22 
 
For this task, both environments received relatively low scores on the negative side, 
with slightly more participants rating Environment A and B at -2 and -1 than in higher 
positive ranges. However, Environment A saw a clear peak at score 2, with 32 votes, 
while Environment B slightly surpassed it with 37 votes. This indicates both 
environments were effective, but Environment B may have fostered a slightly better 
user experience for this task.  
 
 
Figure 6: Collected data on ‘Put Needle on Pen’.  
Inject with User Interface 1  
In this task, participants gave more balanced responses across the scoring spectrum. 
Environment A peaked at score 2 with 20 votes, while Environment B reached 25 votes 
at the same score. Additionally, Environment B generally scored slightly higher across 
positive values (scores 1 and 2), suggesting a marginally more favourable user 
experience compared to Environment A during injection with UI1. Furthermore, 
Environment A has more than double the score of negative votes compared to 
Environment B.  
 
 
Figure 7: Collected data on ‘Inject with UI1’.  
Appendix
127
==PAGE:142==
23 
 
Inject with UI2 
A noticeable difference was observed in this task. Environment B showed a clear 
improvement over Environment A, especially at score 1. Environment A peaked at score 
2 with 29 votes, while Environment B reached 28, which will not be looked at as a 
significant difference. However, Environment B had more votes at score 1 (20 
compared to Environment A’s 11), suggesting users found it easier or more comfortable 
in the more home-like environment. In addition, Environment A has a total score from -2 
to 0 of 14 votes whilst Environment B has a score of 6. Environment B has an overall 
more positive score record than Environment A.  
 
Figure 8: Collected data on ‘Inject with UI1’.  
GSR Movement  
Participants in Environment A gave significantly more positive scores for GSR 
movements. At score 2, Environment A reached 37 votes, while Environment B peaked 
at a lower count, 29. At scores 0 and 1, both environments performed similarly. 
However, Environment B had slightly more responses in the negative range (score -1 
and -2), indicating that the added white noise and plants might have affected 
participants' perceptions of their physiological comfort or ease during the GSR-related 
activity, which is different for the tasks with needle and injection of pens.  
 
Figure 9: Collected data on GSR Movement 
Appendix
128
==PAGE:143==
24 
 
 
Additional Observations 
Across tasks involving hands-on interaction with devices (putting the needle on, 
injecting with UI1 and UI2), Environment B consistently resulted in more positive 
scores, especially around score 1. This suggests that the added elements (plants and 
ambient white noise) might have contributed to lower anxiety, increased focus, or 
greater comfort during practical tasks. However, the exception is the GSR 
questionnaire, where Environment A performed better. One possible interpretation: 
while the environmental modifications made B feel more relaxed during tasks, the noise 
may have had a subtle physiological or psychological impact, making users feel less in 
control or less aware of their bodily responses — hence the lower self-rated GSR 
comfort.  
Environment B had consistently higher counts at score 0 across most tasks. This might 
reflect a segment of participants who felt neither particularly confident nor 
uncomfortable — a kind of cognitive neutrality that could point to a calming or 
dampening effect from the environment. This may be worth exploring further: were 
participants less stimulated (positively or negatively) in B, or did the noise simply mask 
subtle cues they’d usually pick up on?  
Environment A tends to peak sharply at score 2 in every task. This suggests 
polarisation—either participants had a very good experience or they didn’t. In contrast, 
Environment B shows more spread across scores, indicating a potentially more 
balanced or varied experience among users. 
This could reflect a trade-off: while Environment A may produce clearer results (either 
good or bad), Environment B introduces more individual variation.

Appendix 01 - A Moderator’s Guide  
Moderator’s Guide – Formative Usability Study on Pen Injectors Using GSR 
1. Introduction to the Study and Agenda 
Objective: 
• To introduce the study, set expectations, and obtain consent. 
• To establish a baseline Galvanic Skin Response (GSR) reading. 
Moderator Script: 
Welcome the participant: 
‘Hello, and thank you for taking part in this study. My name is [Moderator’s Name], and I 
will be guiding you through today’s session. The co-moderator [Co-moderator’s name] 
will be taking notes, look at the GSR data, start the video, and in general do so the study 
will go smoothly.  
I will read my manuscript aloud to ensure that all participants receive the same 
information exactly as intended. Before we begin, I’d like to briefly explain what we will 
be doing today.’  
Purpose of the Study: 
‘This is a formative usability study that aims to evaluate the usability of different pen 
injectors. We are particularly interested in how people interact with these devices and 
any challenges they might experience when using them. 
Please remember that we are testing the device, not you. There are no right or wrong 
answers, and if you find something confusing or difficult, that is valuable information for 
us. We want you to behave normally and perform the tasks the way you think it should 
be done.’ 
GSR Explanation: 
‘As part of this study, we will also be using a Galvanic Skin Response (GSR) device. This 
sensor measures small changes in skin conductance, which can indicate emotional 
responses or stress levels during the tasks. You will wear the device on your fingers, but 
it hopefully will not interfere with your ability to complete the tasks.’ 
Informed Consent: 
‘Before we start, I need to go through the consent form with you. This document 
explains your rights as a participant, including that you can stop the session at any time 
if you feel uncomfortable. Please take a moment to read and sign the form if you agree 
to participate.’ 
(Wait for participant to sign the consent form.) 
Appendix
131
==PAGE:146==
27 
 
GSR Setup and Baseline Measurement: 
‘I will now place the GSR device on your fingers. We will take a short baseline reading 
while you will answer some demographic questions. This helps us understand your 
normal skin conductance level before we begin the actual study. The GSR will be 
placed on your dominant hand around your wrist and on your index and middle finger. 
Are you left or right handed? Will you please the GSR or should I?’.  
 
2. Demographic Questions 
Objective: 
• To collect basic information about the participant’s experience with pen 
injectors. 
Moderator Instructions: 
‘Before we start, I’d like to ask you a few questions about your background with pen 
injectors.’ 
1. How old are you? 
2. Which gender do you identify as?  
3. Have you currently used pen injectors?  
 If yes: How often do you use it? 
4. Have you ever received training on how to use a pen injector? 
5. Have you ever worked in developing a pen injector?  
6. Do you have any conditions that might affect your ability to use your hands, such 
as arthritis or tremors? 
 
3. Use Scenario 1: Task 1 – Put on Needle 
Objective: 
• To observe how the participant handles and attaches the needle to the pen 
injector. 
Task Instructions (Read Aloud): 
‘You have been living with diabetes and need to take an insulin injection every day to 
manage your condition. You usually take your injection at home, following a routine that 
fits into your daily schedule. 
Today, you will be using a new device (or system) designed to assist with your insulin 
injections. Your goal is to complete your daily injection safely and efficiently while using 
Appendix
132
==PAGE:147==
28 
 
this device. As you go through the process, please think aloud and share any thoughts, 
questions, or concerns you have. 
Now, I’d like you to attach the needle to the pen injector as you normally would. 
Imagine that you are preparing to take an injection. For this you need to take off the pen 
cap.  
Please talk through your thoughts as you complete the task. If you have any difficulties, 
try to solve them as you would in real life, but let me know what you are thinking.’ 
(Observe and take notes on the participant’s actions. Do not assist unless they ask for 
clarification.) 
Probing Questions (if needed): 
• "What are you thinking as you do this?" 
• "Was there anything unexpected about this process?" 
• "What, if anything, would make this easier for you?" 
(Once the participant completes the task, proceed to the questionnaire.) 
 
4. Post Task 1: Put on Needle Questionnaire 
Objective: 
• To gather subjective feedback on Task 1. 
Moderator Instructions: 
‘Now that you have completed this task, I’d like you to answer a few questions about 
your experience.’ 
(Hand the participant the Post Task 1: Put on Needle Questionnaire and allow them to 
complete it.) 
(Once completed, briefly discuss their responses, if necessary.) 
 
5. Use Scenario 2: Task 2.1 – Deliver Injection of UI1 
Objective: 
• To observe the participant injecting into the Diabetic Injection Pad. 
Task Instructions (Read Aloud): 
‘Now, I’d like you to complete the injection process using the pen injector and the 
Diabetic Injection Pad. I will now attach it to your thigh. Will you or should I attach it to 
Appendix
133
==PAGE:148==
29 
 
your thigh?  
When injecting please inject in the ‘H’ half of the injection pad. Please inject as if you 
were administering a real dose, following what you believe to be the correct procedure. 
The dose you should inject is 20 units.’ 
(Observe and take notes on the participant’s technique and any challenges they face.) 
Probing Questions (if needed): 
• "Can you explain why you did that step in this way?" 
• "Did anything feel difficult or unclear?" 
• "What, if anything, would you change about this process?" 
(Once the participant completes the task, proceed to the questionnaire.)

• To gather subjective feedback on Task 2.1. 
Moderator Instructions: 
‘Now that you have completed this injection, I’d like you to answer a few more 
questions about your experience.’ 
(Hand the participant the Post Task 2.1: Deliver Injection Questionnaire and allow them 
to complete it.)  
(Once completed, briefly discuss their responses, if necessary.)

Objective: 
• To assess whether the GSR device affected the participant’s ability to perform 
tasks.

‘Finally, I have a few questions about your experience with the GSR device.’ 
(Hand the participant the Post Test Questionnaire on the GSR and allow them to 
complete it.) 
(After completion, ask follow-up questions as needed.) 
• "Did you notice the GSR device while completing the tasks?" 
• "Did it interfere with your ability to handle the pen injector in any way?" 
• "Would you feel comfortable using a similar device in another study?"

Do you have any final thoughts or suggestions?’ 
(Allow the participant to share any additional feedback.) 
‘Thank you again for your participation. You are now free to leave. Have a great day!’

Appendix 02 – Preparations before next Study Session  
Between every study some preparations must be done. The checklist is:  
- 
Moderator and co-moderator will compare notes and registered use events 
within the data collection.  
- 
The deviations are written down.  
- 
The GSR and video data is exported.  
- 
The GSR and the pens will be wiped down using alcohol wipes.  
- 
The new material for the next session must be prepared (questionnaires, 
consent form etc.)

Appendix 03 – Compliance with IEC 62366-1:2020 and IEC 62366-
2:2016 
According to the standards the test must be complied with:  
Timing and Iteration 
• Formative Evaluations should be conducted early and iteratively throughout the 
medical device development process.  
• These evaluations are performed before Summative Evaluations to ensure user 
interaction problems are identified and addressed.  
 
Evaluation Methods 
The following methods are recommended for conducting Formative Evaluations: 
• Expert Reviews (reviews by usability specialists).  
• Standards Reviews (comparison with established usability guideline).  
• Heuristic Analyses (evaluation against predefined usability principles).  
• Cognitive Walkthroughs (step-by-step evaluation of user interactions).  
• Usability Tests (observing users performing tasks to assess the design).  
 
Data Collection and Analysis 
Formative Evaluation data may include:  
• User comments and feedback.  
• Surveys and focus group discussions.  
• Performance ratings from usability tests.  
The results must be documented in a test report, which includes raw data, 
processed data (e.g., spreadsheets with task performance data), and potentially 
video recordings.  
Evaluation Plan / Protocol 
IEC 62366-1:2020 requires manufacturers to establish a User Interface Evaluation Plan 
that outlines how Formative Evaluations will be conducted. This plan must document: 
• The objectives of Formative Evaluations. 
• The methods to be used. 
• The representative user groups involved. 
• The test environment and conditions.  
 
Stopping Criteria 
Appendix
138
==PAGE:153==
34 
 
• Formative Evaluations do not have strict acceptance criteria but must iterate the 
design until an acceptable usability level is reached.  
• The decision to stop iterating is based on achieving a quality level that ensures 
successful Summative Evaluation.  
 
Noise Levels in Testing Environments 
• The expected noise level of the actual use environment should be considered 
when designing usability tests. 
• If users are expected to operate a medical device in a noisy environment, the 
device should provide visual alarm signals in addition to auditory signals to 
ensure alerts are perceived correctly 
• Example noise levels:  
 Central clinician workstation: Typically 50 dB to 70 dB. 
 Patient rooms: Normally 50 dB to 65 dB.  
 
Test Environment Conditions 
• The test environment should match the intended use environment as closely as 
possible. This means accounting for:  
a. Lighting conditions (e.g., bright vs. dim environments). 
b. Temperature and humidity. 
c. Background noise and potential distractions.  
• In some cases, simulated environments (e.g., hospital settings, ambulances, 
home environments) are used to replicate real-world conditions.  
 
Sample Size Considerations 
Small groups (typically 5–8 participants) are usually enough to uncover major usability 
issues. More participants may be needed if comparing design alternatives statistically.  
 
Representative Users 
Even in formative tests, participants should reflect realistic user groups, especially 
when testing tasks related to safety or performance.

Aspect

Device Name 
NovoPen II.  
Purpose 
Insulin injection pen for subcutaneous administration of 
insulin in diabetes patients.  
Function 
Facilitates accurate dosing and convenient self-
administration of insulin.

User Type 
Description 
Primary Users 
Patients with diabetes requiring insulin therapy, including 
both newly diagnosed and experienced users.

Healthcare professionals (e.g., nurses, endocrinologists, 
GPs) assisting patients in device use.

Caregivers administering insulin on behalf of patients.

Description

Private residences where patients use the device 
independently or with caregiver assistance.

professionals administer insulin.

Conducted at Technolution’s Usability Lab, simulating 
realistic home environments under controlled conditions.

Adjust dial to select the prescribed insulin dose.  
Injection Procedure 
Select injection site, administer insulin correctly.  
Post-Injection Steps 
Dispose of used needle safely, store the NovoPen II.  
Maintenance 
Clean the device, check for wear and tear or malfunctions.

Reusable injection pen with a cartridge-based system.  
Interface 
Mechanical dial for dose selection and push-button for 
injection.  
Feedback Mechanisms 
Audible and tactile feedback for dose selection and 
injection.  
Portability 
Designed for easy carrying and discreet usage.

overdosing.

Risk of accidental needle sticks if not handled properly.

Particularly for elderly users or those with dexterity 
impairments.

Appendix 06 – Potential Risks to Human Subjects 
While this study does not involve injecting into human skin, there are some low-level 
risks associated with handling needles and the test environment. The following 
potential risks and mitigations have been considered:  
Potential Risks 
Risk 
Description 
Mitigation Measures 
Needle Stick Injury 
 
Participants will handle 
real needles when 
preparing and injecting 
into the injection pad. 
 
- Clear step-by-step 
instructions provided.  
- A trained moderator will 
supervise to intervene if 
unsafe handling occurs.  
- Used needles will be 
disposed of immediately in 
a designated sharps 
container. 
Psychological Discomfort 
or Anxiety 
 
Some participants may 
feel uneasy handling 
needles, particularly if 
they are unfamiliar with 
the process. 
 
- Participants will be 
informed before the test 
that they will handle a 
needle but will not inject 
into themselves.  
- They will have the 
opportunity to ask 
questions before starting.  
- The moderator will 
provide reassurance and 
guidance throughout the 
session.  
- Participants can stop the 
test whenever they wish. 
Skin Irritation from GSR 
Sensors 
 
The GSR device requires 
sensors to be attached to 
the skin to measure 
physiological responses, 
with a small chance of 
temporary irritation. 
 
- Sensors will be cleaned 
before and after each use.  
- Participants will be asked 
about pre-existing skin 
sensitivities before 
applying the sensors.  
- If irritation occurs, the 
sensor will be removed 
immediately. 
Environmental Disruptions 
 
The study involves testing 
under different 
environmental conditions, 
which may cause 
temporary discomfort. 
 
- If a participant 
experiences significant 
discomfort, they may 
withdraw from the study. 
 
Appendix
144
==PAGE:159==
40 
 
Data Privacy and 
Confidentiality 
 
The study includes video 
recording and 
physiological data 
collection. 
 
- All collected data will be 
anonymised and securely 
stored to protect 
participant privacy.  
- Data will be used solely 
for research purposes, 
with access restricted to 
the research team.  
- Participants will provide 
explicit consent before any 
recording begins and can 
request data deletion at 
any time. 
Ethical Considerations & Safeguards 
Consideration 
Safeguard Measures 
Informed Consent 
 
Participants will receive detailed study information and 
must provide informed consent before participation. 
Moderator Oversight 
 
A trained moderator will oversee the sessions to ensure 
participant safety and can pause or stop the test if 
needed. 
Right to Withdraw 
 
Participants have the right to withdraw at any time 
without explanation, and any collected data will be 
excluded upon request. 
Compliance 
 
This protocol adheres to IEC 62366-1:2020 and IEC 
62366-2:2016 standards, ensuring ethical usability 
testing with a focus on participant safety and well-
being.

The p-values presented in the Results section were calculated using a two-tailed binomial test.
This non-parametric test was chosen as it is specifically designed for categorical data with two
possible outcomes (’Supports’ vs. ’Contradicts’). The following sections detail the specific input
parameters used for each of the three tests performed.
Test for Environment A
213
==PAGE:228==
Appendix
The test for the data from Environment A alone was performed with the following parameters:
• Total number of trials (n): 90
• Number of ’successes’ (k): 52 (the number of instances supporting the hypothesis)
• Hypothesized probability (p): 0.5 (representing a 50/50 random chance)
This test yielded the result: p = 0.232.
Test for Environment B
The test for the data from Environment B alone was performed with the following parameters:
• Total number of trials (n): 75
• Number of ’successes’ (k): 42
• Hypothesized probability (p): 0.5
This test yielded the result: p = 0.386.
Test for Combined (Pooled) Data
The exploratory test on the pooled data from both environments was performed with the following
parameters:
• Total number of trials (n): 165
• Number of ’successes’ (k): 94
• Hypothesized probability (p): 0.5
This test yielded the result: p = 0.104.
For each of the tests above, the p-value was calculated by statistical software. The value represents
the total probability of observing a result at least as extreme as the one recorded, assuming the
null hypothesis of a 50/50 chance were true.
This involves summing the probabilities of all outcomes in both the upper tail (e.g., for the com-
bined data, getting 94, 95, 96... or more successes) and the equally extreme lower tail of the
binomial distribution. Since all calculated p-values were well above the standard p < 0.05 thresh-
old, the results were determined to be not statistically significant.
